In IPO Cleanup, India Weighs a Refund Plan
In IPO Cleanup, India Weighs a Refund Plan
By Shefali Anand and Sudeep Jain
963 words
2 January 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MUMBAI -- India's capital-markets regulator is taking steps to tame what has been one of the world's wildest
IPO markets, with plans including forcing companies' founders to reimburse small investors for some losses.
The goal is to convince investors that the stock market is a safe place for their savings. In the first 11 months
of 2012, investors pulled out a net total of nearly $2 billion from equity mutual funds, which are used mainly
by individuals.
"There was a feeling in this country that many IPOs are manipulated," U.K. Sinha, chairman of the Securities
& Exchange Board of India, said in an interview.
While all IPOs can be volatile, Indian IPOs had been particularly wild on their first day of trading. In some
cases in 2011, shares jumped as much as 100% from the issue price, giving rise to suspicions about price
manipulation.
SEBI moved to make day trading more costly during the first 10 days after a listing. Before February, day
traders could offset their intraday purchases and sales against each other, paying their broker a net amount
at the end of the day. Under the new rules, which took effect that month, purchases and sales must be settled
separately, requiring investors to tie up more capital.
This rule is applicable to IPOs raising 2.5 billion rupees, or around $46 million, or less, a majority of the IPOs
in India in the last two years.
A separate new rule limits a stock's movement on the first day of trading to a narrow band.
The most controversial regulation is in the proposal stage. In September, SEBI proposed a rule that would
provide refunds for up to 50,000 rupees in IPOs that fall sharply.
The refund would be given if the stock fell more than 20% from its issue price within three months of listing,
even as the broader market was stable or rising. If the market was also falling, the refund would occur if the
stock lost 20 percentage points more than the market.
The founders, or controlling shareholders, would have to buy the investors' shares without drawing on
company funds.
Bankers say the rule, if implemented, could make some companies rethink their plans to sell shares for funds.
"Trying to give some sort of protection . . . is in principle against the spirit" of equity markets, said K. Srinivas,
founder of investment bank Saffron Capital Advisors Pvt.
But Mr. Sinha doesn't think the rule would have a major impact on the IPO market. The refunds would be
capped at 5% of the IPO's total size, he said.
SEBI has yet to decide whether to make this proposal a rule. There is no timetable for a decision.
Less than 5% of India's household savings of around $300 billion are invested in stocks and mutual funds,
according to India's central bank. Indians have preferred to put their money in gold jewelry and real estate.
Some investors moved into stocks after markets began to boom in 2005, but a collapse in prices after 2008,
allegations of wrongdoing and a number of IPOs that fell sharply after listing have turned investors off.
Several stocks listed in the fall of 2011 had particularly large price fluctuations on the first day of trading,
sparking investigations by SEBI. In late September 2011, shares of PG Electroplast Ltd., a maker of
electronic components, were issued at 210 rupees and rose to 484 rupees within a few hours of listing. But
over the next week, the shares lost more than half their value, according to SEBI. In October 2011, shares of
information-services provider Taksheel Solutions Ltd. lost 62% of their value on the day they listed.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
SEBI said it had found evidence of share-price manipulation, among other violations of securities rules, by
these firms. In an order in late 2011, SEBI barred both companies indefinitely from raising equity capital from
the securities market.
Kuntal Kar, PG Electroplast's compliance officer, denied that the company or its founders were involved in
any wrongdoing, and said that the company is considering its legal options. Taksheel didn't respond to
requests for comment.
Individual investors remain wary of equities. India's benchmark Sensex gained 26% in 2012, but remains near
where it traded at the end of 2007, leaving many investors without gains.
An IPO in December by Bharti Infratel Ltd., the telecom-tower arm of Bharti Airtel, raised $756 million, largely
from institutional investors, making it India's biggest IPO in two years. Retail investors bought only 20% of the
stock available to them.
India's government is trying to woo individuals. In September, the finance ministry announced a tax break for
small investors who are investing in equities for the first time.
In October, SEBI announced rules under which it can turn down IPOs if it finds companies have made
inadequate disclosures, or haven't clearly defined how they plan to use the funds. Investment banks working
on IPOs have to declare the performance of all deals they have worked on, a move aimed at shaming banks
with a poor record.
Not all the regulator's moves have been well received.
Venky Panchapagesan, a finance professor at the Indian Institute of Management Bangalore, wrote to SEBI
last year that regulators elsewhere take action only when declines following IPOs are caused by wrongdoing.
"SEBI is abandoning its responsibilities to detect fraud and has decided to penalize all firms whether they
committed fraud or not," Mr. Panchapagesan said in the letter.
Mr. Sinha denied the regulator was trying to fix prices. "The basic idea is to force realistic pricing," he said.

Year-End Review & Outlook of Markets & Finance -- Current Account: After a Wild Year, Time For a Sober Look at Risk
Year-End Review & Outlook of Markets & Finance -- Current Account: After a Wild Year, Time For a
Sober Look at Risk
By Francesco Guerrera
934 words
2 January 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
We all have a period of our lives that we like to think of as "the wild years." For most of us, the degree of
transgressions may have fallen well short of Mick Jagger circa 1972, but in our mind, boy, those were the
days.
Many investors will remember 2012 as such a year. Money managers, hedge funds and even individual
investors and universities all scrambled for that elusive "yield" -- a rate of return that would vault their results
past the measly interest rates paid by Treasurys.
The search took them to ever-riskier places, from junk bonds and mortgage-backed securities to strange
corporate concoctions such as "business development corporations" and "master limited partnerships."
These investments can be found in financial glossaries under the rubric "buyer beware" and could turn out to
be losing bets in years to come.
But in 2012, there were few blowups there -- a sign that, after the risk purge that followed the financial crisis,
the dangers lurking in the financial system remain manageable. For those searching for yield, the past 12
months weren't so much "the year of living dangerously" but "the year of trying to live dangerously."
To be sure, some small investors had an entirely different experience. Uncertain about the economy and
scarred by stock exchanges' persistent inability to run themselves (as shown by the botched listings of
Facebook Inc. and BATS Global Markets Inc.), a cohort of average Joes and Janes pulled money out of
stocks. Not even the stock market's strong gains during the year were enough to entice them back in.
Nevertheless, the majority of the investing world begins 2013 with a strong appetite for risk and a willingness
to look for it in exotic and dangerous corners of the market.
Whether this is the right strategy or just the first step toward inflating another painful bubble will depend on
two factors: the direction of the economy and the price of different assets.
As Fredrik Nerbrand, global head of asset allocation at HSBC Holdings PLC, wrote in a Christmas missive to
clients, "there are two predominant drivers of returns at the moment: the global business cycle and relative
valuations."
On the first point, Europe is mired in a recession, while Asia is likely to contribute its fair share of global
growth. That leaves the U.S. as the differentiating factor for the global economy this year.
U.S. economists love to draw up long lists of "headwinds" (things like Washington policy stalemate, lack of
household income growth and lackluster consumer confidence) and "tailwinds" (a recovering housing market,
an improving trade balance, the energy revolution and the like).
But the fundamental issue centers on the ability of the superloose monetary policy pursued by the Federal
Reserve to stimulate consumers' demand for goods and services, and companies' desire to invest and hire.
Without those elements, the U.S. economic recovery will remain sluggish, patchy and fragile even if
politicians fill the fiscal policy vacuum of the past few years.
As for relative valuations, investors and strategists are stuck in a paradox: Many experts agree that in the
long run, bonds will yield lower returns than in previous decades, but few are prepared to bet that stocks and
other asset classes will do better in the short term.
Mathematics and common sense suggest that with bond yields at record lows and the likelihood of interest
rates rising at some point, fixed income isn't an investment for the ages. In fact, the riskier parts of the bond
market so beloved by fund managers in 2012 -- high-yield debt, mortgage-backed securities and other
structured products -- are even more exposed to rising rates.

--- Page 42 ---
Page 42 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
When the CFA Institute, a global association of investment professionals, polled more than 6,000 members
about their 2013 outlook, half of them said that stocks would provide the best returns this year, up from 41%
last year. Only 8% chose bonds.
But the economic uncertainty and the potential for external shocks such as a big conflagration in the Middle
East, a banking failure in Europe or a crisis in China's wobbly financial sector are keeping many fund
managers and individual investors from plunging headlong into stocks.
In a recent note to clients, Bruce Bittles, chief investment strategist at Robert W. Baird & Co. describes the
dilemma this way: There is no conclusive evidence yet that the bond markets' stellar run is coming to an end
but "the historical pattern . . . suggests it may be getting long in the tooth."
His advice for 2013: "Investors should still focus on managing risks rather than chasing returns."
Fund managers' paths to get to that ill-defined goal vary. But most seem to entail increasing exposure to
stocks while keeping a substantial part of portfolios in high-grade corporate bonds and hedging the risk of
future inflation by buying gold.
On paper, these steady-as-she-goes strategies are sensible. But they are unlikely to satisfy those clients who
demand results every quarter or small investors, pension funds and academic endowments that have
suffered years of below-par returns.
After a year marked by the obsessive search for yield, the investment Holy Grail for 2013 is hidden in the
murky gray zone between risk and return. Happy hunting.
---
Francesco Guerrera is The Wall Street Journal's Money & Investing editor. Write to him at:
currentaccount@wsj.com and follow him on Twitter: @guerreraf72.

Agenda 2013: World: A Brief Look Ahead
Agenda 2013: World: A Brief Look Ahead
1,372 words
2 January 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CHINA
Ascending Amid Scandal,
Leaders Are Under Global Gaze
China's new leadership is expected to provide the first indications of its commitment to restructure the
economy, overhaul foreign policy and combat corruption in 2013, following the completion in March of a
once-a-decade change in power.
Xi Jinping, who became Communist Party chief in November, will become president at the meeting of the
National People's Congress -- the rubber-stamp parliament -- while Li Keqiang, No. 2 in the new party
leadership, is expected to take the reins of the economy as premier.
With the succession complete, the new leadership will face mounting domestic and international pressure to
provide details of how it plans to tackle the vested interests in China's economy, and move from an
export-oriented growth model to one driven by domestic consumption.
In the wake of the Bo Xilai scandal, China's leaders are also expected to face strong calls from the Chinese
public to make systemic changes backing up their rhetoric on corruption and abuse of power. Mr. Bo's trial,
expected at some point in the year, could refocus attention on those issues.
Internationally, meanwhile, foreign governments are anxious to see if the new leadership will try to repair
relations with neighbors alarmed at China's growing military muscle, or maintain the assertive diplomacy that
triggered the U.S. strategic "pivot" toward Asia.
-- Jeremy Page
EGYPT
Battle Over Political Path
Takes Pause for Economic Crisis
Egyptians, weary from two years of political upheaval and violence, begin 2013 facing twin crises -- an
immediate threat of economic ruin and a longer-term battle to form the foundation for the country's fledgling
democracy.
Islamist President Mohammed Morsi's most urgent task is to heal an economy teetering on its own cliff's
edge. In mid-January, Egypt's government will return to consultations over a long-delayed $4.8 billion
International Monetary Fund loan seen by many as the country's last hope to fill a budget deficit set to reach
$13 billion. Mr. Morsi will need to push through tax increases, make serious cuts to Egypt's bloated energy
and food subsidy regime, and possibly shrink its massive bureaucracy.
That will be a tall order for Mr. Morsi, who is also running a deep deficit of political trust. Members of Egypt's
sizable, secular-leaning minority are outraged by Mr. Morsi's Islamist agenda and maximalist attitude toward
power. The Islamists' declining popularity leaves an opening for Egypt's sidelined liberals, who have vowed to
put their best foot forward in parliamentary elections scheduled to take place before the end of February.
The conflict will transform the next legislature into a cultural battleground, as each side fights to make a
controversial new constitution speak for its own camp.
-- Matt Bradley
JAPAN

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In With the Old: A Re-Elected
Prime Minister Vows Shake-Up
Japan enters 2013 with a new leadership determined to shake things up.
First, the economy. Prime Minister Shinzo Abe, who staged a comeback last month after resigning from the
top post in 2007, has declared war on Japan's chronic deflation, thought to be a prime culprit in the country's
two "lost decades" of stagnation.
Mr. Abe is pressing Japan's central bank to adopt a much more muscular monetary policy, including an
inflation target and "unlimited" easing. That rhetoric is already weakening the yen against the dollar, and
economists expect the trend to accelerate this year.
The Abe government is also proposing to boost jobs and output with a big dose of government spending,
casting aside fiscal constraints Japan's previous administration had put into place to corral the country's
runaway debt.
Then, Mr. Abe has said he wants to overhaul Japan's relations with the world, particularly the U.S. and China.
Just what that means is still unclear, but look for increased military spending, a sterner tone vis-a-vis Beijing
and an early visit to President Barack Obama, likely this month. How Mr. Abe handles China is of particular
concern to Asia watchers, because ties between the two countries have frayed badly lately.
-- Phred Dvorak
MYANMAR
Surprise Over Rapid Opening
Gives Way to Watchful Waiting
Myanmar surprised the world over the past 18 months with blockbuster reforms ending decades of harsh
military rule. The big test will come this year, though, when investors and political activists find out if the
changes will stick -- and if Myanmar can start to fulfill its promise as one of the world's hottest new emerging
markets.
Recent troubles have given observers cause for worry. Sectarian violence between minority ethnic-Rohingya
Muslims and majority Buddhists in western Myanmar has left more than 180 people dead since June. A harsh
crackdown on activists protesting against a Chinese-backed copper mine in November raised fears
Myanmar's leaders haven't yet learned to manage dissent without force.
Myanmar leaders say they intend to stay the course, though, and that 2013 will be an even bigger year for
reform. Among other things, they have promised more freedom for the press and bigger investments in
power, communications and tourism infrastructure to make Myanmar more viable for foreign capital.
And then there is the question of whether opposition figures such as Nobel laureate Aung San Suu Kyi will be
permitted to exert serious influence over policy in the country -- or be relegated to secondary roles as former
military officers continue to call the shots.
-- Patrick Barta
AFRICA
Mining Riches Spark Tension,
Testing Will to Attract Investors
Africa's resource riches have drawn scores of foreign investors, notably from China and India. The
high-stakes scramble has also stoked renewed tensions among African neighbors that threaten to become
bigger conflicts in 2013.
Where border tensions are particularly high on the continent, oil and gas is usually involved. Or sometimes
gold. Or water.
Sudan and newly independent South Sudan came to the brink of war last year after repeated clashes along
their ill-defined, oil-rich border. They still haven't agreed on a security pact to restart trans-shipments of crude.
Tanzania and Malawi are bickering over the location of a common border after Malawi awarded a gas
exploration license to a U.K company. And the most recent flare-up in Congo's eastern region involves

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
gold-smuggling rebels who are backed, the United Nations alleges, by Rwanda and Uganda. Both countries
deny the accusations.
Given the importance of food security, frictions are rising as well over water that would flow into the
continent's farmlands. Egypt and Sudan have sparred with Ethiopia over the latter's plans to build a giant dam
on the Blue Nile, a major Nile tributary.
With global levels of energy, minerals and food stocks under pressure, the African continent is well-positioned
to draw foreign investment -- if these countries can finde the political and economic incentives to maintain
peace.
-- Peter Wonacott
MEXICO
Long-Ruling Party Returns,
But Consensus Is Watchword
For Mexico, 2013 marks the return of the Institutional Revolutionary Party, which ruled for most of the 20th
century but was swept to the sidelines in 2000.
President Enrique Pena Nieto, who began his six-year term in December, has an ambitious agenda -- from
quelling Mexico's drug violence to confronting its biggest monopolies. But with no majority in Congress, he
will need to keep the backing of leaders from both left and right to push ahead on a reform agenda.
On the drug war, the president has signaled a shift from taking down big-name drug lords to focusing on
measures to reduce violence.
Leaders will also begin recruiting 10,000-member gendarmes they say will aid the police and military, expand
a new system in which courtroom trials will take place in open courtrooms rather than behind closed doors,
and continue cooperation with the U.S. under the $2 billion Merida Initiative, a security cooperation
agreement.
Mexico's new leadership will try to make the economy its main focus. Mr. Pena Nieto has already passed a
labor-reform law that curbs some union powers and eases the hiring of workers.
-- Nicholas Casey

Year-End Review & Outlook of Markets & Finance: A Tamer but Still 'Wild West' --- Supply-Demand Finds Greater Balance, But Droughts, Gluts...
Year-End Review & Outlook of Markets & Finance: A Tamer but Still 'Wild West' --- Supply-Demand
Finds Greater Balance, But Droughts, Gluts Expose Vulnerabilities
By Liam Pleven
943 words
2 January 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R7
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Long known as the Wild West of asset classes, commodities took a walk on the mild side in 2012. That could
point to a new, potentially calmer stage in the decadelong rally, defined by more balanced markets.
The Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index, which tracks 20 materials from crude oil to lean hogs, fell 1.1%, the
tamest move since it began in 1998. The index had swung by double-digit percentages each of the past five
years and had never risen or fallen by less than 2.7% since the launch.
Producers have ramped up supplies in response to the demand-driven price increases spurred largely by
China's rise. Now, that is damping spikes: Copper, natural gas and gold were among the raw materials that
saw the smallest price moves in years.
The 2012 decline in the index, while modest, was the second consecutive annual drop, the first time that has
happened since the late 1990s. Some analysts said the recent declines mark the end of the supercycle that
began more than a decade ago.
Yet supply constraints can still send prices soaring, as demonstrated by sharp increases in the cost of corn,
soybeans and wheat in 2012 after hot, dry weather damaged crops.
As a result, prices may not fall back to the far-lower levels of a decade ago, if demand stays strong. But they
also may not triple or quadruple again. And while the next round of spikes could still be sharp, they may be
shorter-lived.
"They'll be transient. They won't be structural," said Jeffrey Currie, global head of commodities research at
Goldman Sachs. That will boost the economy, he said. "You don't have this persistent drag on economic
activity."
But even if prices are relatively calm in this new stage, raw materials have the potential to remain a source of
economic stress and political tension.
Energy
The diverging fates of the world's two reigning benchmark crude prices in 2012 illustrate how greater supplies
are already curbing prices in some cases, while tight inventories are keeping them high in others.
U.S. crude prices fell 7%, the first annual decline since 2008, when markets seized amid the global financial
crisis. The drop reflected the impact of new drilling methods that have unlocked vast new supplies of oil and
natural gas in the middle of the U.S.
But Brent crude, the European benchmark that tracks the cost for many foreign buyers, as well as many on
the U.S. coasts, rose 3%, as crude supplies elsewhere in the world remain constrained. The rise, though
small, was striking, given concerns over economic woes in Europe and elsewhere.
The result is a steep discount for buyers who have access to cheaper U.S. crude, which is landlocked to an
extent by inadequate pipeline infrastructure. U.S. crude ended the year at $91.82 a barrel, $19.29 less than
Brent crude, a gap that isn't likely to narrow soon, said Fatih Birol, chief economist at the International Energy
Agency, a group of energy-importing nations. New infrastructure is aiming to ease the bottleneck, but it could
take time to catch up with production.
U.S. natural-gas prices also showed that commodity prices retain the potential for large swings. A glut of the
fuel sent prices down 36% between the end of 2011 and mid-April, but they rebounded as producers pared
back and consumers shifted to the fuel, ending 2012 up 12%.

--- Page 47 ---
Page 47 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Agriculture
Crop prices could be primed for a similar ride in 2013.
A harsh U.S. drought dominated a year of weather problems, and the resulting price increases put stress on
buyers around the world. Wheat prices rose 19%, soybeans climbed 18%, and corn advanced 8%.
In response, farmers around the world are once again planning to ramp up production. If the weather
cooperates, that could push prices back down, perhaps significantly, by the time the harvest comes in next
fall in the U.S., the world's largest corn producer.
"You've got to get there first," said Chris Gadd, an analyst at Macquarie. Animals that are huge consumers of
corn and soybeans need to eat between now and then, and the larder is thin, which could push prices up in
the meantime, especially if harvests in Argentina and Brazil in the first half of the year disappoint. Mr. Gadd
has forecast corn to reach $8.50 a bushel in the March-to-May time frame. By comparison, corn futures for
March delivery settled Monday at $6.9825 a bushel.
Metals
No major commodity has seen the kind of bull run that gold has since the end of 2000. Prices have risen
sixfold in that period, ending each year more valuable than at the outset, and 2012 was no exception; gold
settled up 7%. But fans of the precious metal are nervous. That was the weakest annual gain since 2008,
despite support from continued central-bank buying, another round of Federal Reserve stimulus seen as
weakening the dollar and boosting bullion, and the uncertainty of a hard-fought presidential campaign
followed by down-to-the-wire talks over taxes and spending. Gold fell 5% in the fourth quarter.
The yellow metal still has a large camp of believers. "We think there's still upside potential for 2013," said
Stephen Cucchiaro, chief investment officer at Windhaven Investment Management, which manages more
than $13 billion in assets.

Japan's Lesson for Financial Alchemists
Heard on the Street
Japan's Lesson for Financial Alchemists
By Thorold Barker
329 words
2 January 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
In the Middle Ages, alchemists toiled fruitlessly to transform lead into gold. Things have gotten a little easier
in the 21st Century. Big central banks can boost the value of assets by using printing presses.
But how real are these gains? Shinzo Abe's new policies may give an indication.
The new Japanese prime minister wants the country's central bank to step up its money printing efforts to
reflate the economy. Japan's Nikkei stock index has soared in response, up 20% from the low point hit Nov.
14.
But before U.S.-based investors pile into Japanese stocks, they should stop and consider the currency they
would be buying into.
Take the MAXIS Nikkei 225 Index exchange-traded fund, which is listed in New York under the ticker symbol
NKY. Since the same date in November, it is up only 10.8%.
Why the disconnect? Because some of the gains in the Nikkei have, for U.S. investors, been erased as the
yen has weakened by about 8% against the dollar over the same period. In fact, while the Nikkei notched a
23% gain in 2012, U.S.-based investors would have done better buying, say, the SPDR S&P 500 ETF, traded
under the symbol SPY, than the NKY. The SPY gained almost 12% last year compared with 10.1% for the
NKY.
It is a stark reminder that there are no free lunches when very cheap money boosts financial markets.
The U.S. enjoys some protection as printer of the world's reserve currency. This has been shown by the
dollar's resilience over the past four years or so of ultra-loose monetary policy.
Even that, though, is only relative to the paper currencies of other countries, many of whose central banks
have also been happy to fire up the printing presses.

The world didn't end for gold in 2012, but the financial apocalypse that goldbugs anticipate didn't happen
either. Assuming things don't change much between now and New Year's Day, then the almost 8% increase
in gold prices to $1,654 an ounce wasn't bad. But it was beaten handily by the stock market, with the S&P
500 up 12.5%.
That would make 2012 the first year since 2004 in which stock prices notched up a bigger gain than for gold.
This seems odd given that 2012 saw the Federal Reserve launch "QEternity," or open-ended quantitative
easing. In general, low or negative real interest rates and any threat to the value of the dollar tend to boost
gold prices.
But the fact is that gold has been treading water since peaking at about $1,900 an ounce in September 2011.
Loose monetary policy may provide support for gold as an investment, but high prices also have taken a toll
on an important source of demand: jewelry.
And for all gold's luster, as an investment it lacks an ingredient investors have sought increasingly to
incorporate into their diet this year: dividends.
Indeed, if you incorporate the added income to investors that dividends provide, the S&P 500 actually beat
gold narrowly on a total-return basis as recently as 2009.
Looking ahead, S&P index analyst Howard Silverblatt expects next year to set a record for dividend payouts,
keeping the competitive pressure on gold. Unless, of course, you think the world could end -- it is '13 after all.

Money Funds Brace for Flood --- BlackRock and Federated Prepare for Investor Influx After FDIC Guarantee Ends
Money Funds Brace for Flood --- BlackRock and Federated Prepare for Investor Influx After FDIC
Guarantee Ends
By Kirsten Grind and Dan Fitzpatrick
836 words
20 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BlackRock Inc. and Federated Investors Inc. might bolt the door to shield their funds from a torrent of money
that is expected to flood out of U.S. banks when a federal guarantee lapses on roughly $1.7 trillion in bank
deposits.
The big money managers worry that cash pouring into the $2.65 trillion money-fund industry will further
depress record low yields, hurting investors and making it costlier for managers to operate.
If the influx is large enough, BlackRock and Federated have told investors, some money-market funds could
be closed to new entrants, said people familiar with the conversations.
The private communication is an early sign of the turmoil that could result from the end of a measure installed
during the 2008 financial crisis to encourage small businesses to stay with their local banks. The unlimited
Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. guarantee on no-interest bank accounts is expected to end on Dec. 31 and
revert to $250,000 per depositor for each account category. An effort to extend the program died in the U.S.
Senate last week.
Businesses and municipalities that use the accounts largely for payroll needs are expected to move cash to
money-market funds or large banks. As much as $250 billion in deposits could shift, according to FBR Capital
Markets. More than $220 billion currently protected by the unlimited coverage is held by banks with less than
$50 billion in assets, according to the FDIC.
"It could be a tsunami," said Pete Crane, president of Crane Data LLC, a mutual-fund research firm in
Westborough, Mass.
The big money managers are relaying the plans in response to questions from institutional investors, who say
they are concerned about where they should hold their money. The movement of money threatens to
increase pressure on big firms that already are struggling to boost profits amid a flood of cash and a shift out
of actively-managed mutual funds.
As yields fall, so do the fees money managers can charge investors. U.S. money-market fund yields have
sunk from an average of 4.97% in 2007 to 0.05% today, according to Crane Data.
Pittsburgh-based Federated, the second-largest money-fund company in the U.S., said all its funds remain
open. But "if conditions change, we would certainly do what we could to continue to serve our clients,"
according to a spokeswoman.
New York-based BlackRock, the sixth-largest U.S. money-market fund company, said the company has "no
plans to suspend subscriptions into our Treasury funds at this time."
Some fund managers say they already are seeing inflows. Money-market fund assets have increased for five
straight weeks, rising about 3% over that span, according to the Investment Company Institute, an industry
trade group.
If the surge of cash is big enough, U.S. money-funds could begin to produce negative yields, say industry
experts and fund analysts. The last time money funds in the U.S. produced negative yields was during the
2008 financial crisis.
Money-market funds that invest in U.S. Treasurys likely would be hit hardest by an influx of new cash
because Treasuries are backed by the U.S. government and are expected to be more popular among
investors seeking safety. They account for about $399 billion of money-market fund assets, or about 15% of
the total.

--- Page 51 ---
Page 51 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The wave of new cash is also bad news for big U.S. banks that are already swimming in cash. The spread
between banks' deposit and lending rates is at its lowest point in three years due to low interest rates and
weak demand for loans amid soft economic growth. Big banks have lobbied against an abrupt end to the
guarantee, with one industry group saying it may create "the misperception of instability."
Some small banks already are taking steps to block any outflow of cash, worried that a loss of deposits might
reduce their ability to lend. Joseph Perri, chief executive of Islandia, N.Y.-based Gold Coast Bank, said his
strategy is to spread client funds into a variety of accounts that carry the $250,000 FDIC guarantee and link
them together.
An FDIC spokesman said a business can divide its funds among different accounts as long as the accounts
are held by separate legal entities.
One client that had $8 million covered by the unlimited guarantee recently agreed to split the funds into more
than 10 separate accounts, Mr. Perri said.
Gold Coast, which has $200 million in deposits, once used the blanket guarantee as a marketing tool and at
its height had 15% of deposits under the program. Mr. Perri said he has pushed that number below 5% and
doesn't expect to lose "a nickel" of the remaining amounts. "I would put money on that one," he said.

26-27.

lower yen and hopes for monetary stimulus. Japan's Nikkei was up 1.1%, pushing past 10000 for the first time
since April. Hong Kong's Hang Seng was up 0.5% and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was up 0.4%.

India Adds Sensex Appeal --- Foreign Investors Buying Stock Amid Asian Country's Efforts to Stoke Growth
World Stock Markets
India Adds Sensex Appeal --- Foreign Investors Buying Stock Amid Asian Country's Efforts to Stoke
Growth
By Shefali Anand and Ashutosh Joshi
735 words
19 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
International money managers are loading up on Indian stocks, propelling the country's market to new highs
even as domestic investors remain on the sidelines.
India's 30-company Sensex index has risen 25% so far this year, putting it in the top 10 of the world's
best-performing stock markets. Driving the rally are fund managers who are wagering that the Indian
government's recent efforts to encourage more foreign investment and trim the budget deficit will spur
economic growth -- and bigger corporate profits.
Many investors also expect India's central bank to stoke growth by cutting interest rates.
Those factors underpin "our expectation of growth coming back," says Rana Gupta, senior portfolio manager
for Indian equities at Manulife Asset Management (Singapore) Pte., which manages around $220 billion
globally and is a unit of Canada's Manulife Financial Corp.
Mr. Gupta has been buying Indian stocks since mid-September, when the government announced its new
investment policies.
The wave of optimism among foreign investors is a reversal from last year. Then, investors were fleeing due
to fears that rising inflation and big budget and trade deficits would pummel economic growth. So far this
year, foreign institutional investors have poured $22.5 billion into Indian stocks, compared with outflows of
$357 million in 2011, when the Sensex lost 25%.
In contrast, India's local investors have been dour on Indian stocks, which have provided lower returns than
gold and real estate over the past five years. Equity mutual funds sold to Indians, primarily individuals, have
had net withdrawals of $2 billion this year.
Foreigners' rush into Indian stocks has revived the market for initial public offerings as well. In the biggest IPO
in two years, cellphone-tower operator Bharti Infratel Ltd. raised $756 million.
The Sensex on Tuesday was up 0.6% at 19,364.75 points, just off its 20-month high.
Indian Finance Minister P. Chidambaram starting in September relaxed ownership rules to allow companies
such as Wal-Mart Inc. to open retail stores in India. The government also allowed foreign airlines to invest up
to 49% in Indian airlines. Meanwhile, Mr. Chidambaram aims to cut India's budget deficit by reducing fuel
subsidies.
Increased foreign direct investment could enable India-listed companies to expand sales and boost profits,
which would bolster stocks, investors say.
"If you see more announcements, and clarity about how [these measures] are going to be executed, then this
market could continue to have a run-up," says Medha Samant, Hong Kong based investment director at
Fidelity Worldwide Investment, which manages around $233 billion globally.
To be sure, the Indian government's drive to bolster growth by liberalizing some sectors of the economy has
yet to bear fruit. The country's gross domestic product grew by 5.3% in the July-to-September quarter, the
slowest pace in nearly a decade and down from 8% in the April-to-June quarter of 2011.
The slowdown comes after the Reserve Bank of India, the country's central bank, increased benchmark
interest rates 13 times between March 2010 and October 2011, and then held rates steady at 8% for most of
this year.

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Analysts at Nomura are advising their clients to hold a lower allocation of Indian shares than the allocation of
slightly above 6% in the MSCI Asia Pacific ex Japan Index. "We do not see a meaningful revival in growth in
the near term," the analysts wrote in a research note.
Although tighter monetary policy was a drag on growth, the rate increases helped tame inflation. India's
wholesale prices increased by 7.2% in November, the slowest pace in 10 months.
Now, some analysts believe the RBI will reverse course and start easing, which could help lift stocks. "We
believe that interest rates have peaked," says Ravi Malani, director of equities at Barclays Wealth and
Investment Management in India.
"We'll see margins bottoming out over the next few quarters," says Krishna Kumar, Singapore-based portfolio
manager for Eastspring Investments, which manages $90 billion in assets, and is a unit of U.K.'s Prudential
PLC. Mr. Kumar has added Indian stocks to his portfolio in the past few months.

Wealth Management (A Special Report) --- Investment Advice for 2013: Three advisers offer their predictions -- and strategies -- for next...
Wealth Management (A Special Report) --- Investment Advice for 2013: Three advisers offer their
predictions -- and strategies -- for next year
By Daisy Maxey
1,538 words
17 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
R5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The headlines have been unnerving for investors all year, perhaps never more so than now, with financial
markets bracing for the worst as Congress and President Obama wrangle over a budget deal.
Daisy Maxey, a special writer for Dow Jones Newswires, sat down with three fee-only financial advisers to
discuss what lies ahead for investors and how they can best manage a continuing parade of uncertainties,
from the immediacy of the fiscal cliff to longer-term tax and economic issues.
The panelists were:
-- Greg Plechner, principal and wealth manager, Modera Wealth Management LLC, Westwood, N.J., and
Boston
-- Marjorie Fox, chief executive officer, Fox, Joss & Yankee LLC, Reston, Va.; and
-- Drew Tignanelli, president, Financial Consulate Inc., Hunt Valley, Md.
Here are edited excerpts of their discussion.
MS. MAXEY: It's been another very difficult year for investors. What should they expect from the economy
and markets in the coming year?
MR. PLECHNER: If you ignore the headline news, it's really been a great year for investors across the board.
Whether you were invested domestically, internationally, even real estate, you had double-digit returns. Even
high-quality fixed income has had mid-single-digit returns.
I don't think it's been a very volatile year, by the traditional measures of volatility. There's certainly a lot of
angst, and maybe that's a factor that you need to look at for your clients. But as a long-term investor you
need volatility to be rewarded for investing in riskier assets.
MS. MAXEY: Are you expecting a similar environment in the coming year?
MR. PLECHNER: Yes. It's a matter of managing our clients' expectations of what the fiscal cliff means, what
is the impact of Europe on your portfolio, what could rising interest rates do to your portfolio. All you have
control over is how you react to those scenarios. So that's where we focus a lot of our time.
MS. FOX: If you're looking for headline risk, you can easily find things beyond the fiscal cliff -- the slowdown
in China, the recession in Japan, the euro debt crisis, the recession in many of the euro nations, slowing
corporate profits. But our firm and our clients are long term. I'm optimistic.
MR. TIGNANELLI: We are concerned about 2013. The Federal Reserve is manipulating the bond market. It's
economically illogical that interest rates on two-year, five-year and 10-year Treasurys should be at the rate
they're at when the rate of inflation over the last four years has averaged 2.3%. People are actually losing
money in the bond market after inflation, yet they're fleeing to the bond market as if it's this panacea of
investments. That's a problem, and it's going to blow up sooner or later.
That doesn't mean there aren't great investment opportunities like we found this year, but there's a lot of
danger, and investors have to be alert to that.
MS. MAXEY: A lot of money is still on the sidelines. Should investors be taking on more risk?
MS. FOX: Well, the train left the station about 3 1/2 years ago. The answer is yes. If they haven't already,
they should certainly tiptoe back in. But investors should always have an allocation to equities consistent with
their risk tolerance, so it's not a question of jumping in and jumping out.

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
MS. MAXEY: Are there particular emerging-markets investments you like? Should investors avoid Europe?
MR. TIGNANELLI: I'm not avoiding Europe. But Asia is clearly my favorite. Almost 50% of our equity assets
are focused on Asia. I think small-cap stocks in Japan could be one of the most fascinating buying
opportunities I've seen since 1980. When the price gets down to these kinds of levels, it's hard not to be
invested in Japan. But China is more interesting -- 1.2 billion people who don't have refrigerators, air
conditioners, cars, and they all want them now.
MR. PLECHNER: In the emerging markets, we like Europe and Japan. We do have some exposure to China,
but even though China has this amazing growth opportunity, you can easily make the argument that India has
the same opportunities in terms of number of people, size and demographics. So we don't try to cherry pick
any one country to invest in. It's more to be broadly diversified and take sort of a world market-cap approach
to the countries we invest in.
MS. MAXEY: How do you advise your clients to handle their emotions during market volatility?
MR. PLECHNER: We spend a lot of time in the initial engagements with our clients, getting to know every
detail of their finances, what are your short-term, midterm and long-term cash needs. That gives us the
confidence when we do hit a difficult market cycle to say, look, we've done the planning, we've got a pretty
good handle on where you're at now and there's no need to be reactionary.
Another part of that process is behavior modification. A simple example of that is, you don't necessarily want
to be buying more of something when it's already gone up in value.
MS. MAXEY: What about inflation? Is that something you're worried about going into 2013? Are you
recommending Treasury inflation-protected securities, gold, commodities, corporate bonds?
MR. PLECHNER: One of the most highly correlated investments to changes in the Consumer Price Index is
short-term TIPS. We would just caution that if you're in longer-term TIPS, one of the things that you have to
be willing to accept is somewhat severe interest-rate volatility. So that's something that we've done
defensively; for long-term inflation protection, longer-term TIPS are probably a better investment. We also
invest in commodities and gold. Those two have some characteristics of inflation hedges, but they're
imperfect. They're also not taxed very nicely, so you need to be aware of that when you invest in those. And
you have to be willing to accept volatility.
Over the long term, one of the best ways to protect purchasing power is equities.
MS. MAXEY: Investors are very focused on the fiscal cliff. With so much uncertainty, what are the major tax
concerns for investors in your minds, going into 2013?
MS. FOX: There's concern about ordinary-income tax rates. In response to that, some investors are choosing
to convert their traditional IRA to a Roth IRA, pay the income taxes down at a lower rate and then be out from
under required minimum distributions taxed at a much higher rate down the road.
They're also concerned about higher capital-gains tax rates. Some are selling. That may or may not be a
good idea. If they were going to sell in January, then maybe it's a good strategy to sell in December and avoid
higher capital-gains tax rates. But if they were going to hold the investment for many years, then they're
giving up those dollars used to pay their capital-gains taxes. They're out of the portfolio, and any return they
could have earned on those dollars is no longer there.
They're making charitable bequests. I think the most interesting thing that I have seen is all kinds of
contributions to donor-advised funds. You can make a contribution to a donor-advised fund this year and
actually not make grants to charities until next year or years beyond that. So what they're saying by their
actions is maybe we're not going to see an unlimited charitable deduction. Or they may be saying, 'I realized
capital gains by selling appreciated assets, so I've got to minimize taxes this year by increasing my charitable
deduction.'
MR. TIGNANELLI: If you know you're going to sell an asset, sell it in 2012. But I would never sell a stock that
I have no anticipation of selling and then buy it back a second later just because I believe the capital-gains
rate is going to be lower this year than in the future. Because if I hold a stock until the day I die, it's 0%
capital-gains tax for my family.
I don't like making decisions because of taxes. I make decisions on taxes after I make a good economic
decision, and if there's a tax differential, I go with the better tax differential.
The second thing is you have to consider gifting. You have a $5.12 million gift exclusion this year. Next year,
who knows what it's going to be?

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
MS. FOX: Our advice to our clients is, take a strategy to a point, but don't go whole hog, because it may turn
out that that strategy, with hindsight, is no longer appropriate. So hedge your bets when it comes not only to
investing but to tax strategy as well.

The Weekend Interview with Leszek Balcerowicz: The Anti-Bernanke
The Weekend Interview with Leszek Balcerowicz: The Anti-Bernanke
By Matthew Kaminski
1,876 words
15 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Warsaw -- As an economic crisis manager, Leszek Balcerowicz has few peers. When communism fell in
Europe, he pioneered "shock therapy" to slay hyperinflation and build a free market. In the late 1990s, he
jammed a debt ceiling into his country's constitution, handcuffing future free spenders. When he was
central-bank governor from 2001 to 2007, his hard-money policies avoided a credit boom and likely bust.
Poland was the only country in the European Union to avoid recession in 2009 and has been the
fastest-growing EU economy since. Mr. Balcerowicz dwells little on this achievement. He sounds too busy in
"battle" -- his word -- against bad policy.
"Most problems are the result of bad politics," he says. "In a democracy, you have lots of pressure groups to
expand the state for reasons of money, ideology, etc. Even if they are angels in the government, which is not
the case, if there is not a counterbalance in the form of proponents of limited government, then there will be a
shift toward more statism and ultimately into stagnation and crisis."
Looking around the world, there is no shortage of questionable policies. A series of bailouts for Greece and
others has saved the euro, but who knows for how long. EU leaders closed their summit in Brussels on Friday
by deferring hard decisions on entrenching fiscal discipline and pro-growth policies. Across the Atlantic,
Washington looks no closer to a "fiscal cliff" deal. And the Federal Reserve on Wednesday made a fourth
foray into "quantitative easing" to keep real interest rates low by buying bonds and printing money.
As a former central banker, Mr. Balcerowicz struggles to find the appropriate word for Fed Chairman Ben
Bernanke's latest invention: "Unprecedented," "a complete anathema," "more uncharted waters." He says
such "unconventional" measures trap economies in an unvirtuous cycle. Bankers expect lower interest rates
to spur growth. When that fails, as in Japan, they have no choice but to stick with easing.
"While the benefits of non-conventional [monetary] policies are short lived, the costs grow with time," he says.
"The longer you practice these sorts of policies, the more difficult it is to exit it. Japan is trapped." Anemic
Japan is the prime example, but now the U.S., Britain and potentially the European Central Bank are on the
same road.
If he were in Mr. Bernanke's shoes, Mr. Balcerowicz says he'd rethink the link between easy money and
economic growth. Over time, he says, lower interest rates and money printing presses harm the economy --
though not necessarily or primarily through higher inflation.
First, Bernanke-style policies "weaken incentives for politicians to pursue structural reforms, including fiscal
reforms," he says. "They can maintain large deficits at low current rates." It indulges the preference of many
Western politicians for stimulus spending. It means they don't have to grapple as seriously with difficult
choices, say, on Medicare.
Another unappreciated consequence of easy money, according to Mr. Balcerowicz, is the easing of pressure
on the private economy to restructure. With low interest rates, large companies "can just refinance their
loans," he says. Banks are happy to go along. Adjustments are delayed, markets distorted.
By his reading, the increasingly politicized Fed has in turn warped America's political discourse. The Lehman
collapse did help clean up the financial sector, but not the government. Mr. Balcerowicz marvels that federal
spending is still much higher than before the crisis, which isn't the case in Europe. "The greatest neglect in
the U.S. is fiscal," he says. The dollar lets the U.S. "get a lot of cheap financing to finance bad policies," which
is "dangerous to the world and perhaps dangerous to the U.S."
The Fed model is spreading. Earlier this fall, the European Central Bank announced an equally
unprecedented plan to buy the bonds of distressed euro-zone countries. The bank, in essence, said it was
willing to print any amount of euros to save the single currency.

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Balcerowicz sides with the head of Germany's Bundesbank, the sole dissenter on the ECB board to the
bond-buying scheme. He says it violates EU treaties. "And second, when the Fed is printing money, it is not
buying bonds of distressed states like California -- it's more general, it's spreading it," he says. "The ECB is
engaging in regional policy. I don't think you can justify this."
"So they know better," says Mr. Balcerowicz, about the latest fads in central banking. "Risk premiums are too
high -- according to them! They are above the judgments of the markets. I remember this from socialism: 'We
know better!'"
Mr. Balcerowicz, who is 65, was raised in a state-planned Poland. He got a doctorate in economics, worked
briefly at the Communist Party's Institute of Marxism-Leninism, and advised the Solidarity trade union before
the imposition of martial law in 1981. He came to prominence in 1989 as the father of the "Balcerowicz Plan."
Overnight, prices were freed, subsidies were slashed and the zloty currency was made convertible. It was
harsh medicine, but the Polish economy recovered faster than more gradual reformers in the old Soviet bloc.
Shock or no, Mr. Balcerowicz remains adamant that fixes are best implemented as quickly as possible.
Europe's PIGS -- Portugal, Italy, Greece, Spain -- moved slowly. By contrast, Mr. Balcerowicz offers the
BELLs: Bulgaria, Estonia, Latvia and Lithuania.
These EU countries went through a credit boom-bust after 2009. Their economies tanked, Latvia's alone by
nearly 20% that year. Denied EU bailouts, these governments were forced to adopt harsher measures than
Greece. Public spending was slashed, including for government salaries. The adjustment hurt but recovery
came by 2010. The BELL GDP growth curves are V-shaped. The PIGS decline was less steep, but prolonged
and worse over time.
The systemic changes in the BELLs took a while to work, yet Mr. Balcerowicz says the radical approach has
another, short-run benefit. He calls it the "confidence effect." When markets saw governments implement the
reforms, their borrowing costs dropped fast, while the yields for the PIGS kept rising.
Greece focused on raising taxes, putting off expenditure cuts. They got it backward, says Mr. Balcerowicz. "If
you reduce through reform current spending, which is too excessive, you are far more likely to be successful
with fiscal consolidation than if you increase taxes, which are already too high."
He adds: "Somehow the impression for many people is that increasing taxes is correct and reducing spending
is incorrect. It is ideologically loaded." This applies in Greece, most of Europe and the current debate in the
U.S.
During his various stints in government in Poland, the name Balcerowicz was often a curse word. In the
1990s, he was twice deputy prime minister and led the Freedom Union party. As a pol, his cool and abrasive
style won him little love and cost him votes, even as his policies worked. At the central bank, he took lots of
political heat for his tight monetary policy and wasn't asked to stay on after his term ended in 2007.
Mr. Balcerowicz admits he was an easy scapegoat. "People tend to personalize reforms. I don't mind. I take
responsibility for the reforms I launched." He says he "understands politicians when they give in [on reform],
but I do not accept it." It's up to the proponents of the free market to fight for their ideas and make politicians
aware of the electoral cost of not reforming.
On bailouts, Mr. Balcerowicz strikes an agnostic note. They can mitigate a crisis -- as long as they don't
reduce the pressure to reform. The BELL vs. PIGS comparison suggests the bailouts have slowed reform, but
he notes recent movement in southern Europe to deregulate labor markets, privatize and cut spending -- in
other words, serious steps to spur growth.
"Once the euro has been created," Mr. Balcerowicz says, "it's worth keeping it." The single currency is no
different than the gold standard, "which worked pretty well," he says. In both cases, member countries have
to keep their budget deficits in check and labor markets flexible to stay competitive. Which makes him
cautiously optimistic on the euro.
"It's important to remember that six, eight, 10 years ago Germany was like Italy, and it reformed," he says.
Before Berlin pushed through an overhaul of the welfare state, Germany was called the "sick man of Europe."
"There are no European solutions for the Italians' problem. But there are Italian solutions. Not bailouts, but
better policies."
Why do some countries change for the better in a crisis and others don't? Mr. Balcerowicz puts the "popular
interpretation of the root causes" of the crisis high on the list.
"There is a lot of intellectual confusion," he says. "For example, the financial crisis has happened in the
financial sector. Therefore the reason for the crisis must be something in the financial sector. Sounds logical,
but it's not. It's like saying the reason you sneeze through your nose is your nose."

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The markets didn't "fail" but were distorted by bad policies. He mentions "too big to fail," the Fed's easy
money, Fannie Mae and the housing boom. Those are the hard explanations. "Many people like cheap
moralizing," he says. "What a pleasant feeling to condemn greed. It's popular."
"Generally in the West, intellectuals like to blame the markets," he says. "There is a widespread belief that
crises occur in capitalism mostly. The word crisis is associated with the word capitalism. While if you look in a
comparative way, you see that the largest economic and also human catastrophes happen in non-market
systems, when there's a heavy concentration of political power -- Stalin, Mao, the Khmer Rouge, many other
cases."
Going back to the 19th century, industrializing economies recovered best after a crisis with no or limited
intervention. Yet Keynesians continue to insist that only the state can compensate for the flaws of the market,
he says.
"This idea that markets tend to fall into self-perpetuating crises and only wise government can extract the
country out of this crisis implicitly assumes that you have two kinds of people. Normal people who are
operating in the markets, and better people who work for the state. They deny human nature."
Gathering the essays for his new collection, "Discovering Freedom," Mr. Balcerowicz realized that "you don't
need to read modern economists" to understand what's happening today. Hume, Smith, Hayek and
Tocqueville are all there. He loves Madison's "angels" quote: "If men were angels, no government would be
necessary. If angels were to govern men, neither external nor internal controls on government would be
necessary."
This Polish academic sounds like he might not feel out of place at a U.S. tea party rally. He takes to the idea.
"Their essence is very good. Liberal media try to demonize them, but their instincts are good. Limited
government. This is classic. This is James Madison. This is ultra-American! Absolutely."
---
Mr. Kaminski is a member of the Journal's editorial board.

Stocks' steady string of advances skidded to a halt Thursday, after negotiations on the fiscal cliff cast a

said Jim Baird, chief investment strategist for Plante Moran Financial Advisors, which oversees $7.2 billion in

back from the late-afternoon lows after The Wall Street Journal reported that Mr. Boehner would meet with
Mr. Obama at the White House Thursday evening.

expectations of a decline.

Federal Reserve's new stimulus plan, announced Wednesday, and the agreement by European Union
finance ministers to set up a single supervisor to watch over the Continent's banks. The Stoxx Europe 600
closed 0.4% lower.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average surged 1.7% to an eight-month high as a weakening yen boosted exporters'
shares, while China's Shanghai Composite fell 1% on liquidity concerns, as the central bank was set to
withdraw money from the banking system.
Front-month crude-oil futures for January delivery slipped 1% to settle at $85.89 a barrel, while gold futures
for December delivery shed 1.2% to $1,695.60 a troy ounce. The dollar rose to a nine-month high against the
yen in intraday trading, amid expectations of aggressive stimulus actions following the country's general
elections on Sunday, and edged higher against the euro. Yields on the benchmark U.S. 10-Year Treasury
bond rose to 1.728% as prices fell.

ArcelorMittal Cuts Stake in Project
ArcelorMittal Cuts Stake in Project
By John W. Miller and Alex MacDonald
669 words
14 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ArcelorMittal said Thursday it would scale down its investment in a planned iron ore mine in the Canadian
Arctic, a rich but risky project, as it seeks to cure debt problems and reduce exposure to lackluster commodity
prices and soaring mining costs.
The world's largest steel company by production is selling a 20% stake in Baffinland Iron Mines Corp., which
controls the Mary River iron ore project, to Nunavut Iron Ore Inc., a unit of Iron Ore Holdings LP, which
already owns a 30% stake. The two companies, which declined to discuss the terms of the deal, will now be
joint partners, splitting development costs, although ArcelorMittal will retain marketing rights and will remain
the principal operator.
The deposit, on an island about 1,500 miles south of the North Pole, could yield 18 million metric tons a year
of high grade ore for 21 years, a total value estimated at about $450 billion at current prices. The Canadian
government earlier this month approved construction of the open-pit mine.
ArcelorMittal has estimated that it will cost $5 billion to $7 billion to get the mine started sometime in 2015 or
after, but some analysts say that estimate could skyrocket because the project is fraught with operational
risks. It is dark 24 hours a day between November and January, and it is unclear how many months a year
ships will be able to get the iron ore to market.
"When you get into these desolate places, you're building a port, a railroad, and you have to train the locals,
and that costs you a lot," said Charles Bradford, an analyst with Bradford Research Inc.
Despite talk of the rich resources of Canada's frozen north, few projects have gotten off the ground due to the
region's remoteness and harsh climate. In the territory of Nunavut, where Baffinland operates, there is only
one mine operating, a gold mine run by Agnico-Eagle Mines Ltd.
ArcelorMittal officials said they will decide next year whether to go ahead with the project and will pull the plug
if it looks too expensive. "We have not yet made our final commitment," Lakshmi Mittal, chief executive of
ArcelorMittal, said in an interview.
ArcelorMittal declined to say how much it had already invested. The fair value of the project, based on future
cash flow expectations, is currently about $1.5 billion, according to a calculation by Jefferies & Co. steel
analyst Seth Rosenfeld.
Cost overruns have become one of global mining's biggest headaches. Mining costs are rising at an average
of 10% per year, according to McKinsey & Co., driven by wage inflation and shortages of such things as
mining trucks and the jumbo tires they use.
At the same time, mineral prices aren't keeping up. In March 2011, when ArcelorMittal announced it had
bought a 70% stake, the price of iron ore was $170 per metric ton. Today, it is $120, and some analysts
believe it will keep falling into a range of $80 to $100, according to a recent forecast by UBS.
Mines take four or five years to develop, and some that were commissioned while prices were heading
toward $200 a ton are scheduled to come on stream in the next couple of years, potentially flooding global
markets and depressing prices.
That potentially unprofitable combination has caused BHP Billiton Ltd. to suspend an expansion to its
Olympic Dam mine in Australia and shareholders to push out Anglo American PLC CEO Cynthia Carroll.
ArcelorMittal debt totaled $23.2 billion in the third quarter, up $1.2 billion over the previous quarter. Two rating
firms have downgraded the company to junk status -- the first time it has fallen below investment grade --
citing earnings insufficient to pay off debt on schedule.

--- Page 64 ---
Page 64 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In response, the company has been selling noncore assets and cutting costs to reduce net debt.

The Federal Reserve's new stimulus program failed to excite skeptical investors, who bid stocks higher for
several hours before erasing those gains to end the day largely unchanged.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished with a decline of 2.99 points, or 0.02%, to 13245.45, snapping a

The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.64 points, or 0.04%, to 1428.48, extending its winning

fell against the euro but jumped against the yen. Treasurys fell, pushing the yield on the benchmark 10-year

Stocks began the day quietly, trading around the flat line ahead of the Fed's announcement that, as
expected, it would buy $45 billion in Treasurys each month, on top of the $40 billion in mortgage-backed
securities it purchases each month. The program will replace its current stimulus program known as
Operation Twist, which ends this month.
However, the central bank also said it would keep interest rates at near-zero rates as long as the
unemployment rate remains above 6.5% and inflation stays in check, surprising investors who didn't expect
the Fed to explicitly tie its interest-rate policy to unemployment.
The Fed decision sent the Dow up as many as 81 points. But the blue chips relinquished those gains later in
the afternoon as investors reassessed the implications of the decision.
"If they're going to keep their accommodative policy until the unemployment rate gets to 6.5%, they'll be at it
for a long time," said Keith Bliss, senior vice president at Cuttone Co. "It's clear to me that they're trying to
inflate assets and really gin up consumer spending.. . .I expect we'll get that initial pop, but then people will
step back and say, 'What is the real efficacy of this for the real economy?' And it's getting less and less."
Separately, investors continued to watch negotiations on the "fiscal cliff," a bundle of tax increases and
spending cuts set to take place in the new year, provided Congress doesn't act to avert those changes. On

14.53, while DuPont added 61 cents, or 1.4%, to 44.30, to lead the Dow gains. DuPont announced a $1
billion stock repurchase program.
European markets were marginally higher after Greece's successful bond buyback. The Stoxx Europe 600
inched up 0.1% to stretch its win streak to eight sessions, and to finish at its highest level since May 2011.
Greece's borrowing costs fell after bids for the debt exceeded the <euro>30 billion targeted.

products affirmed its 2012 earnings outlook.

Buffett, purchased 9,200 of its larger Class A shares at $131,000 a share from the estate of a longtime
shareholder.

Labor Market Plods Forward --- Concerns About 'Fiscal Cliff' Don't Squelch Hiring but Lackluster Pace Reflects Weakness in Economy
Labor Market Plods Forward --- Concerns About 'Fiscal Cliff' Don't Squelch Hiring but Lackluster Pace
Reflects Weakness in Economy
By Neil Shah
904 words
8 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Hiring increased at a slow but steady pace at American businesses last month, despite a massive storm and
growing fears of a brewing fiscal crisis in Washington.
The Labor Department's latest job-market overview showed employers added 146,000 jobs in November.
That is better than the previous two months, which were revised down, but still too weak to get many of
America's 4.8 million long-term unemployed back to work.
The biggest surprise in the report was the government's pronouncement that Superstorm Sandy didn't
significantly affect the findings. Economists had expected the storm, which hit the Northeast in late October,
to slice tens of thousands of jobs from the national tally. Some expressed skepticism that the report didn't
reflect that more.
The unemployment rate, drawn from a different survey than the payroll count, fell to 7.7% from 7.9%, the
lowest level since December 2008 -- but mainly because people left the workforce. Part of that may be
because people surveyed said they were unavailable to work while busy cleaning up storm damage.
The report Friday helped drive stocks to a one-month high, with investors interpreting the news as more
confirmation that the economy is improving. Gold initially tumbled and Treasurys fell, while the Dow Jones
Industrial Average gained 81.09 points, or 0.6%, to 13155.13, led by bank stocks. The yield on the 10-year
Treasury note, which rises as prices fall, jumped to 1.627%, from 1.581% on Thursday.
Friday's report suggests the jobs market hasn't been blown off course by worries about the "fiscal cliff," a
cluster of tax increases and spending cuts set to hit early next year unless Congress makes a deal to resolve
it. But November's figures also reveal a job market that is improving too slowly to significantly lower
unemployment or boost the economy's overall growth, which faces headwinds. Economists estimate growth
in the current quarter is anemic, running less than 1%.
"The sluggish recovery continues," said Torsten Slok, chief international economist at Deutsche Bank. "The
big relief is that the fiscal cliff did not have the negative impact on hiring that we all feared."
Most of November's job growth was concentrated in service industries, including the retail and health care
sectors.
One place hiring last month was Mongo's Grill, a restaurant in Boise, Idaho, that opened just before
Thanksgiving. The owners, who operate several other eateries, hired 107 workers to staff their Boise
operation. Merlyn Knight, one of the owners, said in recent months he has grown cautious about further
investments like this, largely because of worries about higher taxes. A jump in taxes for households with
incomes over $250,000 -- which the Obama administration is pushing for -- could hurt his own income, Mr.
Knight said, as well as those of diners at his restaurants.
"We will not make a decision to sign anything or make a long-term commitment until after this shakes out,"
Mr. Knight said about the budget standoff. "We're going to be very risk averse."
The Labor Department report revealed a divided job market: Service jobs, such as in restaurants and stores,
fueled much of the job growth, with retail employment alone adding more than 50,000 jobs. Meanwhile, the
goods-producing part of the economy and construction stumbled. Construction employment fell by 20,000 last
month and manufacturing lost 7,000 jobs.
Santo LaMantia, chief executive of Shure Inc., a closely held maker of wireless microphones, is feeling better
about the recovery and optimistic about 2013. "We have been strategic, more so than conservative," in hiring,
Mr. LaMantia said. "We've hired people as needed in order to put the right talent in place."

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, November's tepid jobs report is the latest sign the economy is losing some momentum. Much of the third
quarter's 2.7% annualized growth rate stemmed from businesses building inventories, which will be less
necessary in the fourth quarter, crimping growth. Macroeconomic Advisers, a consulting firm, expects growth
of only 0.5% in the fourth quarter, the slowest pace since the first quarter of 2011.
While any slowdown could prove temporary, even a brief stall will hurt job creation -- one of the main factors
keeping consumers confident. "It takes a little time for the souring expectations to translate into slow job
growth," said Ben Herzon, an economist at Macroeconomic Advisers.
Consumers have been much more upbeat than businesses, thanks to improvements in jobs, home prices and
the stock market, but they also are showing signs of pulling back.
A survey of consumer confidence in early December, released Friday, suggests Americans are less upbeat.
The Thomson-Reuters/University of Michigan consumer sentiment index's preliminary reading for December
slumped to 74.5 compared with 82.7 at the end of last month. Readings hovered around 90 before the
recession.
With the unemployment rate high and job growth sluggish, the report didn't change expectations that Federal
Reserve officials at next week's meeting will decide to continue the central bank's substantial bond-buying
program.

Gold Hasn't Been So Precious
Commodities Report
Gold Hasn't Been So Precious
By Matt Day and Tatyana Shumsky
427 words
8 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold investors have yet to experience some holiday cheer.
The precious metal is down 5% from the October highs hit after the Federal Reserve announced its last round
of stimulus. The decline represents disappointment among investors who wagered that an increase in
monetary easing, by central banks in Europe and Japan as well as by the Fed, would spur inflation or at
least fears of rising consumer prices, traders and analysts said.
Neither has materialized as some anticipated in the five years the Fed has been in easing mode, so more
investors are giving up on gold, said Dave Meger, director of metals trading with Vision Financial Markets.
Gold's recent fall represents "the unwinding of this trade," he said. During the week ended Tuesday, money
managers cut bets on higher gold prices by the most since early March, according to the Commodity Futures
Trading Commission.
The past two weeks of trading have been rocky, with the market rattled by investors putting on big bearish
bets in gold options and one frantic bout of selling in what is usually a quiet period of trading in Asia. Options
market activity suggests investors are seeking to guard against further declines.
Gold eked out a gain of 0.2% on Friday, with the front-month contract, for December delivery, rising $3.70, to
settle at $1,704 a troy ounce, as traders closed out bets ahead of next week's Fed meeting. Gold for
February delivery, the most actively traded contract, rose $3.70, or 0.2%, to $1,705.50 an ounce.
Many analysts expect the Fed to extend "Operation Twist" -- purchases of long-term Treasurys and the sale
of short-term notes to keep long-term interest rates low -- but not by enough to guarantee a gold rally going
into year-end. Still, analysts with UBS said an "aggressive move" by the Fed could draw buyers back to the
market.
"That would potentially turn the tide," said Kurt Pfafflin, a senior broker with commodities brokerage Daniels
Trading.
Gold is up 8.8% in 2012, and money managers are cashing out "to capture those gains before year-end,"
said Global Hunter Securities analyst Jeffrey Wright. The potential of tax increases set to take effect in
January also has pushed some investors to exit. Also, buying of physical gold from top consumers India and
China has cooled off from last year's pace, eroding a traditional support for prices.

OFF DUTY --- Style & Fashion -- 20 Odd Questions: Aerin Lauder --- The granddaughter of Estee Lauder and founder of her own lifestyle bra...
OFF DUTY --- Style & Fashion -- 20 Odd Questions: Aerin Lauder --- The granddaughter of Estee
Lauder and founder of her own lifestyle brand on beauty, basics and (not eating) breakfast
Edited from an interview by Caroline Tell
1,142 words
8 December 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
'The idea Estee had was that a woman knows what other women want, and it's so true," said Aerin Lauder,
sitting in her bright Madison Avenue office, where the modern art and Art Deco furniture evoke a private salon
more than a numbers-crunching HQ. It's exactly this truism that Ms. Lauder, 42, has applied to her lifestyle
brand, Aerin, launched in early fall.
Ms. Lauder may be a New York society fixture and the heiress to a global cosmetics empire, but she's also a
working mother who appreciates a natural-looking and effortless makeup palette, a woman who likes a
beautiful pouch to hold her favorite shades and a fashion lover who seeks pieces that are stylish but not silly.
Though Ms. Lauder began Aerin with collections of makeup and beauty-related accessories like batik-linen
cosmetics bags, she's since branched out in several different directions. Next spring, she'll be selling shoes,
jewelry made in collaboration with Erickson Beamon and sunglasses with Oliver Peoples, as well as furniture,
lighting and home accessories -- all of which reflect her pragmatic-yet-pretty ethos.
More immediately, Aerin has a line of holiday makeup that includes shimmery eye shadows and berry-hued
lipsticks and glosses sized to fit inside evening bags. Also new: a collection of festive home objects featuring
colorful agate coasters and gold-dipped vases and candy bowls. "It's all about the idea of embellishments
and multiple uses," said Ms. Lauder. "Different containers you can fill with nuts, Christmas candies or flowers.
I can't live with without them."
---
The biggest beauty myth is that everyone looks better without makeup. That's not true. Makeup is important.
My fashion philosophy is quite traditional and understated. I never wear too much jewelry. I never wear a ton
of pattern. Sometimes I'll go big but it never feels right. At one CFDA Awards, I wore a fantastic short, neon
dress and unexpected shoes. It looked great, but I just didn't feel like me.
My New Year's resolution is to not drink as much Diet Coke. It's so bad for you and I've been drinking it since
college.
Aging is beautiful. I love laugh lines. It means you've had a good life. The most beautiful women -- Audrey
Hepburn, Lauren Hutton, Ali MacGraw -- all embraced the aging process. Do I believe in plastic surgery?
Yes, if something is wrong and you can modify it. For certain people, it's right.
My favorite scent is anything floral. Tuberose and gardenia are my favorite flowers.
I'm really badabout breakfast. I have a coffee in the morning and the remnants of my children's breakfast. At
lunch, I love salad or sushi but I'm not a fish person. I've never craved grilled salmon. I like hamburgers, I like
chicken. I've never liked pasta.
My holiday party style is simple and feminine -- a great pair of pants and a silk top with a piece of statement

I never take off my Cartier Tank watch. I got it about 17 years ago for Christmas and I've always loved it. It's
as beautiful as it was when I first got it. It's timeless, and looks good with everything from a dress to a bathing

The five favorite piecesin my closet are black Repetto ballet slippers, DL 1961 jeans, a Stella McCartney
blazer in navy or black, Hermes cashmere scarves and probably a Stella McCartney white silk shirt.

The biggest beauty lesson my grandmother taught me is that you only have one face, so take care of it. She
taught me that everything starts with a good skin care routine. She also taught me that while something might
be fun to look at in a magazine, it doesn't mean you have to wear it.
My beauty routine is very effortless. Sometimes I get up and don't brush my hair. I put on foundation, a little
