My definition of paradise is to take a bath at the end of a long day with Jo Malone's Red Roses Bath Oil. I

I don't do facials that often, but I do get a mani/pedi once a week. I stay neutral with colors -- Essie shades

Overheard: Wealthier Households

The Wall Street Journal

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Good news! U.S. households are approaching the level of wealth they had before the financial crisis cracked
their nest eggs. But there are catches.
The Federal Reserve reported Thursday that Americans' net worth climbed to $64.8 trillion in the third
quarter, compared with $63 trillion three months earlier. The way things are going, 2007's peak of $67.3
trillion ought to be retaken next year.
There are some reasons to spare the confetti, however. First, adjusting for inflation, the 2007 peak rises to
$75.1 trillion. Also, the population has grown about 13 million over the past five years. Third-quarter per
capita net worth, at $206,000, was well below the $250,000 reached in 2007.
Of course, $206,000 per capita is a lot more money than most families have. And what really drove the
recovery in net worth was a rebound in the value of stocks and other financial holdings, or assets rich
households are more likely to hold than middle-class ones. It is impossible to calculate, but the median
family's inflation-adjusted net worth must be far below its old level. The recent turn higher in home values is
good news as are the roughly two million more people working now than a year ago. But there still is a way to
go before people feel as comfortable as before the recession struck.
---
The market can be a volatile beast. A stock that gets hammered one day often rebounds the next. This
makes Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold's experience even more dispiriting. Wednesday, the mining
company announced two oil deals with a presentation memorably described by Evy Hambro of BlackRock as
"one of the worst teleconferences I've ever heard to justify a deal." The market agreed: Freeport's stock fell
16% that day. Problem is it lost a further 4.2% Thursday. It's down $7.1 billion since Tuesday's close, more
than the $6.9 billion it is paying for Plains Exploration & Production. And investors still don't think it's cheap.

Gold Fields: Divide and Conquer
Heard on the Street
Gold Fields: Divide and Conquer
By Liam Denning
502 words
30 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Gold Fields is going short on South Africa and long on irony. The miner said Thursday that it will spin off a
new entity called Sibanye Gold.
"Sibanye" means "we are one" in the Xhosa language -- which is about as far away from capturing the
underlying rationale for the move as possible. Still, it at least hints at the fact that Gold Fields' fundamental
problems are shared by gold miners everywhere.
Sibanye houses two old, declining South African mines. After it is gone, South Africa will account for just 13%
of Gold Fields' output, down from 47% today.
Cutting exposure to South Africa is a no-brainer in valuation terms. The three big miners there -- Gold Fields,
Harmony Gold Mining and AngloGold Ashanti -- trade at an average forward price/earnings multiple of 7.3
times, a 39% discount to the Philadelphia Gold and Silver index. Besides this year's violent labor unrest in
South Africa, that gap also reflects structurally higher costs for older, labor-intensive mines there. Wages
account for 53% of South African cash costs, compared with a global average of 25%, according to Credit
Suisse.
So for all the underlying message of togetherness, Gold Fields wants to diversify.
More important, this move is the latest manifestation of a shake-up in global gold. All miners are grappling
with a major problem: how to sell themselves to indifferent investors. Once you get past the disclaimers, the
first slide in Gold Fields' presentation explaining the spinoff shows how gold prices have more than doubled
since the start of 2006, while gold-mining stocks have gone nowhere.
Increasing costs have eroded miners' leverage to rising gold prices, traditionally the core of their appeal.
Poor spending discipline has destroyed value, raised debt levels and limited dividends. Meanwhile,
exchange-traded funds have given gold investors an easy alternative.
Miners have been shaking up executive ranks and trying various strategies to win back investors' favor. Apart
from Gold Fields, Barrick Gold has spun off African assets into a separately listed company. It now is in talks
to sell its remaining majority stake in African Barrick Gold to state-owned China National Gold. Indeed, given
their hunger for global resources and low cost of capital, such entities could end up being natural buyers of
Sibanye and any other South African assets that fall loose.
These are steps in the right direction, but the pressure on miners looks likely to increase. High gold prices
encourage more output, the same as any other commodity.
Surveying major gold miners representing nearly half of global output, Pawel Rajszel, analyst at Veritas
Investment Research, says that their aggregate production forecasts add up to growth of 8% a year out to
2016.
Such an increase would undermine the gold price, even as cost inflation squeezes margins from the other
direction. In that respect, the miners are all in the same boat.

Obama Needs to 'Pull a Rubin' on the U.S. Dollar
Obama Needs to 'Pull a Rubin' on the U.S. Dollar
By Andy Kessler
1,224 words
24 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Facing stubbornly high unemployment and slow growth, swelling deficits and a divided Congress, President
Obama is surely scrambling for an economic elixir. He has often cited the economy of the 1990s during the
administration of his Democratic predecessor, Bill Clinton, as his ideal. President Clinton managed to keep
the economy moving ahead briskly despite repeated foreign currency crises -- and despite raising taxes,
which should have been an economic drag.
That seems to be Mr. Obama's plan. As he has said repeatedly, he wants to increase tax rates on
"millionaires and billionaires" to "the same rate we had when Bill Clinton was president" -- 39.6% -- "the same
rate we had when our economy created nearly 23 million new jobs."
Dream on. Given increases in state, local, payroll and other taxes since the 1990s, the effective rate is
considerably higher. In California -- the home of venture capital and of many job creators -- the top marginal
income-tax rate would exceed 50% thanks to the state's new 13.3% rate. The top capital gains rate in the
Golden State, if Mr. Obama gets his way, would rise past 37% -- the scheduled increase on Jan. 1 to 20%
plus 3.8% in ObamaCare plus the 13.3%, since the state taxes capital gains as ordinary income.
Here's a better idea. If Mr. Obama wants the economy to get some of that Clinton mojo, he needs to pull a
Rubin.
Robert Rubin, who took over as Treasury Secretary in January 1995 after 26 years at Goldman Sachs,
understood a thing or two about markets. In particular, he knew that during the inflationary 1970s, weak
dollars flowed into commodities instead of stocks and ventures that were vulnerable to shifts in the value of
the currency. During the Reagan era, Mr. Rubin and Goldman Sachs thrived by learning that a strong dollar
attracts productive investment that drives a growing economy.
Under President Clinton, Treasury Secretary Rubin told everyone who asked that "the U.S. supports a strong
dollar." And he put the country's money where his mouth was, pushing a strong-dollar policy that included
working with central banks to keep the dollar's value up by buying and selling currencies and advocating free
trade. During Mr. Rubin's nearly five-year tenure at Treasury, the dollar price of oil and gold dropped; the
unemployment rate declined to 4.3% from 5.6%; and the stock market more than doubled. The Clinton
economic legacy exists primarily because Robert Rubin acted on what he learned during the 1980s.
But somewhere in the dozen years since the end of the Rubin era, the country has lost its way. As low-end
manufacturing jobs began to be outsourced to Japan, then Taiwan and now China, support for a strong dollar
dissipated as the mistaken view took hold that a weak dollar would boost exports and return U.S.
manufacturing to its glory days.
And we've had a bad run of Treasury secretaries. Alcoa executive Paul O'Neill didn't last long under
President George W. Bush, and in 2008 he admitted to Bloomberg TV: "When I was secretary of the Treasury
I was not supposed to say anything but 'strong dollar, strong dollar.' I argued then and would argue now that
the idea of a strong-dollar policy is a vacuous notion." His replacement, railroad executive John Snow, was
for a "sound dollar," not a strong dollar, saying in 2003: "You want people to have confidence in your
currency." He watched as the dollar dropped 46% against gold.
When the dollar is devalued, it flows into fixed assets and out of financial instruments and productive
endeavors -- into gold instead of Google, oil instead of risky startups. And as the world saw in the 2000s, far
too much into housing.
Henry Paulson, Mr. Rubin's partner at Goldman Sachs, tried to change the tone. He started at Treasury in
July 2006, and after just four months had the dollar up 20% -- although he would spend most of his tenure
battling the housing and banking crises caused by a loose, dollar-debasing Federal Reserve.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Then came former New York Federal Reserve President Timothy Geithner. This Treasury secretary
understands that "the U.S. supports a strong dollar," even telling the Senate Finance Committee in February
2010: "That particular phrase and commitment of policy was first written in my office at the Treasury
Department in 1995" -- for his boss, Robert Rubin.
But Mr. Geithner didn't back it up. On April 26, 2011, he told the Council on Foreign Relations: "Our policy
has been and always will be, as long as at least I'm in this job, that a strong dollar is in our interest as a
country. We will never embrace a strategy of trying to weaken our currency to try to gain economic
advantage." Yet a week later, at a meeting of the U.S.-China Business Council, he said: "It would be better
for the world, more fair to us, and we think in China's interests, to let the exchange rate appreciate more
rapidly." In other words, forget the strong dollar.
It is no wonder that dollars have fled to fixed assets like gold, bidding the price up to $1700 an ounce from
$900 during the Obama administration. Meanwhile, investible cash sits on the sidelines or offshore, waiting
for better dollar-based returns.
Mr. Obama doesn't need Congress to kick-start investment in the U.S. It takes a strong dollar and a new
Treasury secretary with credibility as a start. That person will need to persuade Federal Reserve Chairman
Ben Bernanke or his eventual replacement to end what has come to be called "QE Infinity" -- investors' belief
that the Fed will just keep printing dollars and debasing their value by quantitative easing.
This is a key moment. High tech from Apple to Cisco to Google will benefit from a strong dollar as memory
and storage and displays get cheaper and foreigners have to pony up more yuan and euros for an iPad Mini.
Pharma and retail and many other industries will benefit as well.
But the real kicker in 2013 will be fracking-induced lower energy costs in the U.S. This is not about heating
homes or cheaper driving, though that will help. It is about bringing back aluminum and chemical factories
that, seeking lower natural-gas costs, were driven to build factories in Saudi Arabia and other oil-producing
countries. A rising dollar -- versus other currencies but especially against the price of oil and natural gas -- will
make a decision to build in the U.S. an easy one. Without support for the dollar, foreign capital will stay
offshore until investing in the U.S. feels safe.
A strong dollar has already proven under Presidents Reagan and Clinton to increase investment and then
jobs and then profits and then more investment. A weak dollar will delay an investment boom and continue
the country's current plodding path. Inquiring minds and investors wonder which it will be.
---
Mr. Kessler, a former hedge-fund manager, is the author most recently of "Eat People: And Other
Unapologetic Rules for Game-Changing Entrepreneurs" (Portfolio, 2011).

Treasury Owns the U.S. Gold Reserve
Letters to the Editor
Treasury Owns the U.S. Gold Reserve
401 words
23 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In regard to John F. Prusiecki's Nov. 9 letter "Deleveraging the Fed With a Golden Plan": Reasonable people
apparently still believe that the Federal Reserve can escape the eventual accounting consequences of its
quantitative easings (total assets now worth about $2.832 trillion versus capital of $54.8 billion) simply by
marking up the value of the U.S. gold reserve from the official price of $42.22 per ounce to current market
value.
The gold reserve is about 261.5 million ounces, and the total spot market value is $453.5 billion. At the
official price, however, the reserve is worth about $11.041 billion. Such a markup would create a bookkeeping
profit of about $442 billion and would reduce the Fed's leverage ratio to a more acceptable level (about 6 to
1) from the current level of about 52 to 1. Unfortunately for this line of argument, the Gold Reserve Act of
Jan. 30, 1934, transferred legal title to the Fed's former gold holdings to the Treasury.
That fact cuts off the Fed's bookkeeping escape route by using the gold reserve. To pay for the 1934 gold
transfer, the Treasury issued non-transferable gold certificates to the Fed. But now, if the Treasury sold all its
gold holdings, for example, it could satisfy its obligations under current law by tendering about $11.041 billion
to the Fed and keeping the remaining $442 billion for itself. That is because, under the gold-clause cases
that the U.S. Supreme Court decided in the 1930s, the Fed would be barred from claiming more than $11.041
billion (the official price value of 261.5 million ounces) from the Treasury. Congress would have to appropriate
any additional transfer of gold or dollars to the Fed.
I am a former Federal Reserve Bank attorney and I have no current authority to speak for the Federal
Reserve System or the Treasury. Still, I doubt that the current general counsel of either the board of
governors or the Treasury would disagree in public with the conclusions stated here about who really owns
the U.S. gold reserve. I think it is the Treasury Department, not the Federal Reserve.
Walker F. Todd
Research Fellow
American Institute for Economic Research
Chagrin Falls, Ohio

City News -- Metro Money: This Job Is for The Birds
City News -- Metro Money: This Job Is for The Birds
By Anne Kadet
1,085 words
17 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Every Thanksgiving, New Yorkers face the big poultry question: supermarket turkey, or heritage bird from the
farmer's market? You know the drill. The supermarket bird was likely fed a diet of toxic waste and forced to
work in an iPad factory, while the local turkey was spoon-fed by angels and taught in a Montessori school.
The decision seems easy, until you compare prices. Supermarket turkey is practically free -- $1.39 a pound
on average, according to the American Farm Bureau Federation. Bespoke turkey, meanwhile, starts at $3.50
a pound, and often costs twice that. My local gourmet grocer is selling free-range, vegetarian-fed heritage
turkeys for $9.50 a pound -- putting the birds in the same league as lobster, caviar and Mast Brothers
Chocolate. Yes, I chose the wrong profession. I should have been a turkey. I mean, a turkey farmer. I'd be
rich!
Or maybe not. I recently spent an afternoon with Richard Biezynski at Northwind Farms up in Tivoli, N.Y. Mr.
Biezynski, whose methods have been widely emulated, may be the original hipster farmer. Born and raised in
Queens, he's been producing antibiotic-free, cage-free, guilt-free turkey since 1981.
Northwind Farms is 197 hilltop acres just east of the Hudson. It's a gorgeous, scenic spot; you can see the
snow clouds rolling in from miles away. Mr. Biezynski met me in the driveway dressed in a sharp Prada suit
and wielding a gold-plated pitchfork. Kidding! He wore muddy boots and was fixing the battery on his beat-up
old truck. We headed straight for the turkeys.
Half the flock had already been "processed" for the holidays, but hundreds remained. If you've never seen a
flock of turkeys, they look like little mechanical dinosaurs, breaking out in mad sprints and generally making a
huge racket.
"They don't know where they're going," said Mr. Biezynski over the din. "They're teenagers, kids." The turkey
yard is a nasty, peckish playground, though Mr. Biezynski and others insist the birds make fine pets, being
naturally social and curious. And they're not as dumb as legend has it. They crane their necks in the rain,
says Mr. Biezynski, so the water runs off and they don't get soaked.
Yes, Mr. Biezynski knows a lot about turkey. But when it comes to poultry economics, he's hard to pin down.
What's the feed bill? "My wife keeps it away from me. It gives me a heart attack!" What's his profit per bird?
"Don't even go there!" He says he's calculated his earnings at 25 cents an hour. I'm still not sure if he was
being serious.
I later tried to piece the picture together by doing a little ag research and chatting with Roger Mastrude,
founder of the Heritage Turkey Foundation. Turns out, the upscale turkey business is crazy capital-intensive.
Take the chicks, which Mr. Biezynski orders from a hatchery and picks up at the Tivoli post office. While the
standard industrial issue might cost $1 each, heritage breeds start at $7.
Feed is the real killer. Big producers may pay 20 cents a pound for the standard corn and soy mix, but a small
farmer buying organic pays roughly double that. And heritage turkeys grow at half the speed of industrial
birds, meaning they consume twice as much food. The greedy upscale turkey gobbles about 90 pounds of
food prior to slaughter -- $36 worth of grain.
Then there's labor, utilities, facilities, insurance and taxes -- expenses that ag schools say add another 30%
to 40% to the cost of production. A farmer like Mr. Biezynski, then, might spend $55 to produce a standard
16-pound Thanksgiving bird, or $3.40 a pound. That's scary close to the $3.75-$4.50 a pound he charges at
the farmer's market.
Well, money isn't everything; isn't farm life satisfying? Mr. Biezynski, 63, is clearly proud of his operation, and
rightly so. Over the years, he's added several barns and a solar-energy plant, devised a self-sustaining crop

--- Page 82 ---
Page 82 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
system and expanded his menagerie to include cows and pigs. The place has a good vibe; animals literally
frolic in the pasture like Disney characters. But the way Mr. Biezynski describes it, farm life is hell on earth.
For one thing, everyone's after his turkeys. When the Hudson Valley was mostly farmland, there were few
predators. Now the woods are lousy with coyotes, bobcats, mink, fishers and vicious, vicious weasels. One
morning, Mr. Biezynski woke to find 25 baby turkey feet scattered around the pasture. The culprit? Skunks.
Then there's the neighbors. Poultry can escape, and Mr. Biezynski says he's seen drivers "accidentally"
bump his birds in the road, throw them in the trunk, and take off.
The work is grinding. The tractor hurts his back. The turkeys weigh a ton. In the days leading up to
Thanksgiving, the family is up before dawn, slitting throats in a small cinderblock building. They'll have killed
hundreds of birds before the work day ends 16 hours later. "We're all on medication. We're exhausted," he
says.
The customers can be worse than the turkeys. While Mr. Biezynski is proud that city folks make the two-hour
drive in their SUVs to fetch a bird, they can be demanding, "We're dealing with a customer who got his
13.5-pound turkey but wants 14 pounds. We get loads of those calls!"
One fall, the pressure got so bad, Mr. Biezynski just up and quit. "I'm done," he told his crew. "Everyone go
get a job!" But the orders kept coming in, Mr. Biezynski kept making exceptions and soon he was back. "I
can't stop working," he says.
There is, of course, one reliable reward. Mr. Biezynski loves feasting on turkey parmesan, turkey meatballs
and open-face sandwiches. Come Thanksgiving, he'll be gobbling -- you guessed it. He likes the dark meat.
Not that this cheers him up. In his gloomy final email to me, Mr. Biezynski provided more detail on his many,
many expenses, and concluded with a grim forecast: "So after all is said and done this year, who knows how
much in the red we might be?"

Stocks Seize Day but Decline on the Week

Stocks posted their biggest daily gain since the U.S. presidential election, after lawmakers said budget talks

The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 45.93 points, or 0.4%, to 12588.31, reversing a morning slide of as
much as 71 points, but declined for the fourth consecutive week, the longest such slump since August 2011.
It fell 1.8% on the week.

turned investors' attention to the looming "fiscal cliff," a series of tax increases and automatic spending cuts
that would go into effect on Jan. 1 if a budget deal isn't reached.

Ranking Senate and House of Representatives members from both political parties said opening negotiations

On the economic front, U.S. industrial production fell 0.4% last month, the Federal Reserve reported, bucking
economists' expectation for a 0.2% increase. Superstorm Sandy's battering of the East Coast depressed

Government Puts a Hold

Spain's government unveiled a new offer of relief for indebted homeowners facing eviction, but groups
representing mortgage holders called it insufficient.
Under a decree aimed at resolving "the most urgent cases," courts will freeze home evictions for
lower-income families, including the long-term unemployed who no longer qualify for jobless benefits, for the
next two years. The government also will set up a pool of low-rent housing for the needy, Deputy Prime
Minister Soraya Saenz de Santamaria said.
The measures are being rushed out amid a popular uproar over a wave of evictions that has become one of
Spain's most visible social problems five years after a property bust. Rising unemployment -- it recently
surpassed 25% -- has left hundreds of thousands of families struggling to make mortgage payments.
-- Christopher Bjork

CHINA
Beijing Lowers Fuel Prices
In Bid to Boost Economy
China announced cuts to heavily regulated fuel prices, injecting further stimulus into the economy, which has
shown signs of recovery in recent months.
The adjustments to retail gasoline and diesel prices may lead to lower input costs across the board and help
ease inflation, already at its lowest in nearly three years.
China will cut gasoline prices by 310 yuan ($49.75) and diesel prices by 300 yuan a metric ton, effective
Friday, the National Development and Reform Commission, the country's top economic planning body, said
Thursday. This represents cuts of 3.2% and 3.4%, respectively, from current average gasoline- and
diesel-price ceilings.

Gold Drops As Demand Pulls Back
Commodities Report
Gold Drops As Demand Pulls Back
By Tatyana Shumsky
401 words
16 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
India is the largest net importer of gold. Articles about gold prices on the In the Markets page on Aug. 7 and
Nov. 16, 2012, incorrectly called it the largest importer. Switzerland imported more gold than India last year,
though much of it was re-exported. By contrast, most of the gold imported by India stayed in that country.
(WSJ May 2, 2013)
Gold prices dropped on signs that demand for the precious metal is sagging.
Global gold demand fell 11% during the third quarter compared with a year ago, the World Gold Council
said Thursday. The gold-mining industry group cited an economic slowdown in China, slumping global
growth and a lack of inflation. Inflation traditionally spurs investments in gold as a way to protect against
rising prices.
The drop in purchases was "sobering news" for the market, said Adam Klopfenstein, senior market strategist
with Archer Financial Services.
The front-month contract, for November delivery, lost $16.20, or 0.9%, to settle at $1,713.30 a troy ounce on
the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The World Gold Council report showed demand sank across regions and across methods to invest in the
precious metal. Two of Europe's biggest gold-buying nations, Germany and Switzerland, reduced purchases
of gold bars and coins by half, the council said.
Meanwhile, China, the world's largest producer of the precious metal, cut its gold consumption by 8%.
Chinese investors, who tend to favor gold jewelry, cut purchases of gold baubles by 6%, to 130.7 metric
tons.
"It was weaker than I would've thought, and you did take a step back," said Bill O'Neill, principal with
broker-trader Logic Advisors, of the demand drop.
One of the few bright spots in the report came from India, the world's largest importer of the precious metal.
Purchases there rose 9%, to 223.1 tons, during the third quarter.
However, gold sales in India more recently have been disappointing, market participants said.
The five-day Hindi Diwali festival, which concluded this week, typically touches off strong demand. But Diwali
gold sales reached about 70 metric tons this year, well short of the 100 metric tons recorded in 2011, said
Prithviraj Kothari, director of RidhiSidhi Bullion Ltd., a gold-trading firm.

In India, A Golden Trading Day
In India, A Golden Trading Day
By Khushita Vasant
324 words
9 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
India's stock exchanges are trying to cash in on Indians' love for gold.
The nation's two national stock exchanges plan to hold a special trading session Sunday just for gold
exchange-traded funds to mark the Hindu festival of Dhanteras. The day is considered auspicious for buying
gold and is the first day of the five-day festival of Diwali, which is associated with wealth and the giving of
gifts.
The Bombay Stock Exchange and the National Stock Exchange will allow trading in gold ETFs from 11 a.m.
to 3.30 p.m., almost as long as a weekday stock-trading session. In addition, both stock exchanges have
waived transaction fees on all trades.
A gold ETF is like a stock, and its price movement is meant to correspond to the price of physical gold.
The event is part of a broader effort by the exchanges to tap into Indians' voracious appetite for gold but to
steer investors away from physical gold toward "paper gold," or exchange-traded funds.
Indian stock exchanges have long held special stock-trading sessions during Diwali because it is believed
that any business conducted then will bring good fortune. But the daylong gold sessions are new.
Last year, when Dhanteras fell on a Monday, the stock exchanges extended the regular stock-trading session
by 3 1/2 hours to allow trading only for gold ETFs.
Indians have traditionally been huge buyers of physical gold, mainly for jewelry, making India among the
largest gold-importing countries in the world.
In recent years, however, as gold prices have risen sharply, individuals have turned to it as an investment
and hedge against inflation.
As of September, gold ETFs in India had assets of $2 billion, nearly 40% higher than the same period a year
ago, according to the National Stock Exchange.

Deleveraging the Fed With a Golden Plan
Letters to the Editor
Deleveraging the Fed With a Golden Plan
170 words
9 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Robert M. Sussman (Letters, Oct. 31) says that a mere 50 basis-point increase in yield on the Federal
Reserve's portfolio would wipe out its equity, which he says is leveraged at 40 to 1, and the Fed would then
need taxpayer funding to prop it up. The Fed, however, carries $11.037 billion of gold on its balance sheet,
but at a value of only $42.22 per ounce. If it carried the gold at close to its current value (i.e., about $1,710),
its balance sheet would show about $447 billion of gold with a corresponding increase in equity resulting in
leverage of less than 6 to 1.
Before going to the taxpayers, the Fed should first write up its gold to current value.
John F. Prusiecki
Chicago
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Treasury Owns the U.S. Gold Reserve" -- WSJ November 23, 2012)

Organizing to Battle Drug-Resistant TB
Organizing to Battle Drug-Resistant TB
By Desmond Tutu
834 words
8 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Cape Town, South Africa -- Leaders of southern African nations are meeting in Mozambique this week to plan
an unprecedented attack on an ancient foe: tuberculosis. Fueled by new drug-resistant strains and the
HIV/AIDS pandemic, TB is spreading like wildfire through South Africa's mines and to the world beyond.
Extensively drug-resistant TB -- a deadlier form of the airborne disease, one that can overpower nearly all
available drugs -- first emerged in South Africa in 2008. It has since been discovered in 84 countries,
including the United States and much of Europe. With a concerted and coordinated response from
governments, the mining industry, international development partners and civil society, we can stop this
disease in its tracks.
The way to start is by strengthening efforts to detect and treat TB in southern Africa's mines. With $2.5 trillion
in mineral reserves, South Africa has the largest mining sector in the world. Migrant workers from neighboring
countries come here in droves to extract gold, diamonds, chromite, vanadium and coal.
The work can be devastatingly toxic for the body. South Africa's 500,000 mine workers have the highest
recorded rate of TB among any demographic in the world. The conditions that give rise to such epidemics
also fed recent strikes in our platinum mines, which descended into tragedy and dozens of deaths in August
in the country's Marikana region.
Working conditions in the mines are inhumane and untenable. While underground, miners breathe in silica
dust that can inflame and damage their lungs and weaken their immune system. Because they live and work
in cramped spaces, the cough of one person infected with TB can circulate for hours while others breathe it
in. Miners are also at risk of being infected with both HIV and TB. Sex work is common surrounding the
all-male hostels where miners live. When HIV damages the immune system, a dormant TB infection quickly
advances into contagious TB disease.
Miners then return home and spread the disease to their families and communities. In this way,
mine-associated TB gives rise to 760,000 new cases annually in Africa. Inadequate and interrupted treatment
can then lead to drug-resistant strains of the disease.
This takes a heavy economic toll. According to a study commissioned by the Southern African Development
Community, TB costs South Africa alone $886 million each year in health-care costs and in impoverishment
when family providers are too sick to work, or die.
That is why SADC leaders representing 15 nations have resolved to strengthen TB control efforts in Africa's
mining sector. This summer, they signed a declaration committing themselves to address the epidemic. The
concrete steps they pledged to take include identifying TB in migrant workers, improving cross-border
treatment for them and their communities, and creating an effective system for compensating workers for
illness acquired on the job.
African governments should be commended for taking this initiative, but they cannot succeed alone. The
mining industry and labor unions, for instance, will benefit greatly from healthier employees -- and a
cooperative effort to safeguard the health of miners and their communities could also foster reconciliation
between the groups.
Many international development partners, such as the U.S. government, are engaged in the global fight
against TB. But budget deficits have forced cuts to TB-control programs. As President Obama embarks on
his second term, I hope the resolve of SADC governments to fight TB reinvigorates America's commitments.
The World Bank also must play a leading role. On his first trip as the president of the World Bank, Dr. Jim Kim
-- himself a TB expert -- traveled to South Africa in September. He said that the World Bank has "the world's
deepest bench of development experts" for confronting the challenges posed by the unchecked spread of TB
in our mines, and can "convene all the relevant partners and help build a truly regional response to an

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
epidemic that does not respect borders." Dr. Kim should match these inspiring words with bold action and
create a new financing stream targeted on TB -- just as the World Bank has done for malaria and HIV/AIDS.
The study commissioned by the SADC estimates that South Africa alone could save $783 million year in
increased productivity and lower health costs if proven inventions are implemented to stop TB in the mining
sector. Throughout the region, avoiding the disease could help millions of Africans climb out of poverty in our
lifetimes.
After knowing for more than a century that the South African mining industry is rich with TB, we finally have
the political will in the region to create an emergency response to arrest its spread. For the health of the
region -- and to protect a world at greater risk from TB -- we must seize the opportunity and end this disease.
---
Archbishop Tutu is the archbishop emeritus of Cape Town, South Africa.
Subscribe to WSJ: http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires

JOHANNESBURG -- South Africa's mining industry continued to suffer from labor disruptions Tuesday with
one mine closing even as another producer resumed operations after workers returned from a strike.
The country's largest gold producer, AngloGold Ashanti Ltd., temporarily closed one mine due to a sit-in by
workers, while employees returned to work at second-largest producer Gold Fields Ltd.
A long strike also continues at Anglo American Platinum Ltd., the country's largest platinum producer, and
industry experts cautioned that wider labor unrest could resume. Even though the majority of strikes have
ended, they said the deals made between workers and the companies are on shaky ground, meaning South
Africa's crucial mining industry may face more strikes.
The uncertainty risks further damage to Africa's largest economy and could lead to more job losses in a
country where unemployment already sits at 25%. The strikes have pressured South Africa's already sluggish
growth rate, which the finance ministry forecasts at 2.5% for 2012, down from a previous estimate of 2.7%
growth.
Gold Fields said Tuesday that production has resumed at its KDC East mine, ending a 23-day wildcat strike.
All three of its mines previously hit by strikes are now producing.
"We are confident that for now production will start ramping up. But we can't say with 100% certainty that
strikes won't flare up again," said Gold Fields spokesman Sven Lunsche.
As Gold Fields resumed work, AngloGold said workers at its Mponeng mine staged a second sit-in in a week
Monday, and the company was forced to close that mine temporarily. All of AngloGold's mines were shut after
employees went on a wildcat strike at the end of September, but workers returned Oct. 26.
"Since then, a strike, a series of underground sit-ins, acts of vandalism and threats of violence have
prevented the mine from operating normally," the company said.
Anglo American Platinum, the last major South African precious-metal producer still trying to end a
monthslong labor dispute, is holding meetings with unions as the wildcat strike continues to cut platinum
output and cost the company and the country important revenue.
South Africa's export revenue will be 12.5 billion rand ($1.43 billion) lower this year as a result of the strikes,
which caused a loss of 10.1 billion rand in gold-and-platinum revenue and 180 million rand in coal revenue
so far, said South Africa's finance minister, Pravin Gordhan, Tuesday in response to a question in parliament.
"A terrible precedent has been set," said National Union of Mineworkers spokesman Lesiba Seshoka. "We
hope this is the end of labor unrest but don't think it is. Miners see that they can strike illegally, get rehired
and demand a wage increase."
Anglo Platinum already dismissed 12,000 employees at its mines as a result of the dispute as workers
continue to push for higher wages. Despite the dismissals, the company said employees can return and get a
2,000 rand bonus. Meetings continue between worker representatives and Anglo Platinum to try to end the
strike that has shut just over a third of the company's production since September. Anglo Platinum
representatives weren't available to comment on the issue.
Gold Fields had dismissed 8,100 employees at the KDC East mine after they missed a deadline to return to
work, but following negotiations with the NUM most of those have been reinstated.
The three largest gold producers -- AngloGold, Gold Fields and Harmony Gold Mining Co. -- agreed to
increase mine workers' salaries by between 1.5% and 2%. Anglo Platinum has said it won't engage in wage
talks.

"The pressure for more job losses in the wake of higher wage inflation and declining production makes
further job losses likely," analysts at Credit Suisse said in a note. They said that gold-mining firms have
already cut 36,000 permanent jobs in the past six years. "We anticipate labor relations to continue to be tense
and [to] see aggressive wage demands."

Stocks Scratch Out a Gain
Monday's Markets
Stocks Scratch Out a Gain
By Matt Jarzemsky
471 words
6 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks rose on the eve of the presidential election, rebounding from a selloff Friday that had capped the Dow
industrials' second consecutive weekly loss.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 19.28 points, or 0.1%, to 13112.44. The blue-chip index had posted
its biggest one-day loss since Oct. 23 on Friday.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index tacked on 3.06 points, or 0.2%, to 1417.26. Materials and energy
shares led the advance alongside gains in commodity prices, while the utilities sector lagged behind.
The Nasdaq Composite Index added 17.53 points, or 0.6%, to 2999.66.
Volume on major stock exchanges operated by NYSE Euronext and Nasdaq OMX Group Inc. was 20%
below the average this year and the lowest since Oct. 8.
"The key is obviously the election," said Omar Aguilar, chief investment officer of equities at Charles Schwab
Investment Management, which oversees more than $250 billion. "The anticipation of the election, I think, got
people in wait-and-see mode for the day."
Entergy slid $3.33, or 4.7%, to $67.90, pacing declines among utility shares in the S&P 500, after the power
distributor to southern states reported third-quarter revenue that fell short of analysts' expectations.
Apple rose 7.82, or 1.4%, to 584.62, after saying it had sold three million iPads in three days since the launch
of its new iPad Mini and fourth-generation iPad. That was double its previous first-weekend milestone.
The Institute for Supply Management's nonmanufacturing purchasing managers' index indicated
service-sector activity expanding at a slower pace last month than in September. The reading fell just shy of
economists' forecasts.
Most European markets closed lower, with the Stoxx Europe 600 shedding 0.6%, amid more weak data out of
Spain. Spanish jobless claims rose in October from the previous month, although the pace of gains slowed
from year-earlier levels. In addition, the European Central Bank is reviewing whether it is treating Spanish
government bills too generously when they are presented as collateral for loans.
Meanwhile, investors awaited a Greek parliamentary vote Wednesday on austerity measures linked to the
country's bailout and future in the euro zone. Spain's IBEX index fell 1.9%, while Greece's FTSE/Athex index
climbed 3.9%.
Asian markets were mostly lower, on the back of Friday's losses in the U.S. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average
lost 0.5% and China's Shanghai Composite slipped 0.1%.
Crude-oil prices added 0.9%, to settle at $85.65 a barrel, while gold increased 0.5%, to settle at $1,682.20 a
troy ounce. The price of the 10-year Treasury note rose, pushing the yield down to 1.684%.

Overheard
Heard on the Street
Overheard
254 words
5 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal

English

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
The havoc wreaked by superstorm Sandy has left many East Coast Americans scrambling for food, gasoline
-- and money. Big banks have deployed temporary ATMs around New York City to help cope with what
appears to be hoarding of greenbacks by residents.
This must be galling to hard-core goldbugs foreseeing a collapse of the world's reserve currency.
Compounding this, the price of gold fell 2.3% Friday to back below $1,700 a troy ounce.
Gold's problem is recovery. Friday's payroll numbers showed jobs growth accelerated in October. As
important were big upward revisions in the August and September data, suggesting a positive trend taking
hold. Last week's October retail sales reports were also positive.
Signs of strength in the economy undermine the case for endless quantitative easing on the part of the
Federal Reserve. This, in turn, bolsters the outlook for interest rates and the dollar -- and undermines the
case for hedging with gold.
Investment demand now accounts for 40% of the gold market. Yet its luster has dimmed on this front. In the
past decade, one-year returns on gold have been negative on only 6.5% of trading days, making this a rare
occurrence. Yet six of the past seven days have scored negatively on this score.
There is always potential for bad news -- such as the fiscal cliff -- to derail the economic recovery. But for
now, gold's tailwind has died down.

End of Election Is Win for Markets
Abreast of the Market
End of Election Is Win for Markets
By Tom Lauricella
818 words
5 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tuesday's presidential and congressional elections can't come soon enough for investors hoping for some
clarity on crucial U.S. fiscal-policy questions.
While the winners have a sizable list of long-term issues to tackle when it comes to government spending,
taxes and regulation, many investors will be watching the results with an eye toward a more pressing
concern: reaching a deal to avoid the so-called fiscal cliff, the year-end expiration of some $670 billion worth
of tax cuts and spending that economists say could send the U.S. back into recession.
"The fiscal cliff becomes front and center," says Michael Jones, chief investment officer at RiverFront
Investment Group in Richmond, Va., which manages $3.3 billion.
Many investors expect a close contest but for President Barack Obama to win. They also anticipate that
Republicans will keep a majority in the House of Representatives and that Democrats will retain slim control
of the Senate, which would set up an instant battle over whether to keep or kill the tax cuts and spending.
A win by Mitt Romney is seen as taking fiscal-cliff concerns off the table, as both sides of the aisle are
expected to agree to an extension that would defer the need for immediate fixes. But a Romney victory also
could introduce fresh unknowns, particularly when it comes to the end of Federal Reserve Chairman Ben
Bernanke's term in 2014.
Regardless of the outcome, investors are looking forward to having the election over, and not only from the
standpoint of investment decisions. Uncertainty over tax and regulatory policy is widely seen as a damper on
economic activity in recent months.
"Knowing who the president is will allow investors to again focus on the fundamentals and put this uncertainty
behind them," says Matthew Rubin, head of the investment-strategy group at Neuberger Berman, which
manages $203 billion.
Stocks have posted healthy gains for the year. The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished Friday's session
up 7.2% so far in 2012, while the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index is up 12.5%.
Some say a win for the president could spark safe-haven buying of U.S. Treasurys and the U.S. dollar and a
selloff in riskier investments such as stocks. That is because House Republicans have refused to back any
revenue increases and President Obama has said he would veto any extension of upper-income tax cuts,
notes John Bellows, investment management strategy analyst at Western Asset Management, which
manages $459 billion.
A prolonged fight, while potentially damaging, may not be as significant to the markets as last summer's
debt-ceiling debacle, even if it is accompanied by another downgrade to the government's credit rating, says
Benjamin Pace, chief investment officer at Deutsche Bank's private wealth management group. "It's not like
the shock of last summer when we woke up. . .and realized we were no longer triple-A rated," he said.
A Romney win, by contrast "would lead to a pretty-good sized relief rally" for stocks and other risky
investments, predicts RiverFront's Mr. Jones.
Within the stock market, Mr. Jones and others see defense stocks as instant beneficiaries of a win by Mr.
Romney, in large part because the sector likely would avoid the automatic spending cuts that a failure to
reach agreement on the fiscal-cliff issues would trigger.
In addition, Mr. Jones believes a win by Mr. Romney would be a boost for banks because it would raise
expectations of a repeal of the Dodd Frank financial-oversight law. The re-election of President Obama likely
would hurt stocks of high-end retailers because of expectations of higher taxes on the wealthy, he adds.

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
J.P. Morgan stock-market strategist Thomas Lee also expects a victory by Mr. Romney to benefit energy and
health-care stocks because he would be expected to loosen rules on energy exploration and push to repeal
health-care reform. Small-company stocks also could get a boost on an expected pickup in
mergers-and-acquisition activity.
Meanwhile, a re-election of President Obama would be a positive for hospital stocks as health care reforms
are preserved, Mr. Lee says.
Some investors see a win by Mr. Romney as introducing new uncertainties, particularly as it relates to the
course of monetary policy, says Carlos Asilis, chief investment officer at Glovista Investments, which
manages $500 million. Mr. Romney has criticized the Fed's Mr. Bernanke and any successor could enact
more restrictive policy, he says.
Mr. Asilis says he would play a win by Mr. Romney by betting on a decline in gold prices and a rise in the
dollar. He would play a win by Mr. Obama by emphasizing "reflation trades" such as bullish bets on housing
and commodities and bets against the U.S. dollar.

Corporate News: Anglo Chief Fell Victim to Cost Overruns --- Pressure Grew as Mine Expenses Rose, Iron-Ore Prices Fell
Corporate News: Anglo Chief Fell Victim to Cost Overruns --- Pressure Grew as Mine Expenses Rose,
Iron-Ore Prices Fell
By John W. Miller
956 words
31 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Anglo American PLC Chief Executive Cynthia Carroll's unexpected resignation last week signals that
shareholders are willing to punish mining executives who can't rein in spiraling costs at the long-term,
multibillion-dollar projects that have long characterized the industry, even if most cost inflation is beyond a
company's control.
Over the last decade, buoyed by rising commodity prices and surging demand, mining companies embarked
on ambitious plans to develop mines in remote regions of the world, confident that the investment would
result in higher sales and profits.
But with prices of iron ore falling and the costs to develop mines rising, the biggest projects face increased
scrutiny by companies and shareholders. Analysts say they don't expect any new $20 billion-plus "megamine"
projects to be announced in the next year. And companies, including BHP Billiton Ltd., Rio Tinto PLC, Xstrata
PLC and Vale SA, have scaled back or put projects on hold.
What set apart Mrs. Carroll and prompted increasing shareholder pressure was continued investment in the
face of slowing demand and cost overruns. In particular, shareholders cite an iron-ore project in Brazil called
Minas Rio that has cost almost $10 billion, twice its current estimated value, and is three years behind
schedule.
"She never should have gone there," says Ian Liddle, chief investment officer at Allan Gray, a South African
investment firm that was one of the institutional shareholders pushing for Mrs. Carroll's ouster. "It was a bet
on the right commodity, but it came too late in the economic cycle and it cost too much."
Mrs. Carroll, who earned plaudits for improving safety and for recording record profits in 2008 and 2011 at the
world's fifth-biggest miner, said last week that it was a "great honor" to lead Anglo American and that she was
proud of everything the company has achieved in her 5 1/2-year tenure. "I feel that the time will be right to
hand over to a successor who can build further on the strong foundations we have created," she said in a
statement Friday announcing her resignation. She will step down once a replacement is found.
An Anglo spokesman said Mrs. Carroll was traveling this week and wasn't available for additional comment.
Mrs. Carroll "changed the way employees are treated in deep-level mines in South Africa," says Patrice
Rossouw, head of equities for Sanlam Investment Management, a South African shareholder. "But now the
focus needs to be delivering good operation performance."
Mrs. Carroll sold off the London-based company's gold operations in 2008 to focus on key industrial
commodities like iron ore, coal, platinum and copper. Mr. Liddle and some other shareholders and analysts
now advocate splitting up the company into assets based on region and commodity -- for example, one
focused on South African platinum and another on South American iron ore -- that they say would have more
cumulative value to shareholders than the current Anglo American.
By 2011, the company had 100 projects, representing $85 billion worth of investment, with most centered on
raw materials needed to make steel and construct new buildings, cars and appliances for parts of Asia
undergoing industrialization.
For a time, margins mining those minerals were so high that "you could muck it up and still be saved by a
20% annual appreciation in metal price," says Paul Gait, an analyst with Sanford C. Bernstein & Co.
That is no longer the case. Iron-ore prices have dropped over 20% to $110 a ton from over $135 in 2011,
which has made the payoff for the Minas Rio iron-ore project in Brazil more difficult.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Anglo bought the mine in two phases in 2007 and 2008 for $4.6 billion from a company owned by Brazilian
billionaire Eike Batista. In pure mining terms, the Minas Rio is a geological jewel: 5.8 billion tons of reserves
at an extremely high 68% iron-ore grade, a mineral treasure chest worth over half a trillion dollars if the entire
reserve is mined and sold at current prices.
The cost of developing the mine, which includes building a processing plant and a 320-mile pipeline to ship
iron ore to a port, was estimated at $2.4 billion, but will end up costing at least more than twice as much and
be three years behind its 2011 target date. While a company has the power to choose suppliers and manage
inventories of trucks, tires and other key equipment, analysts and company officials say the biggest
contributors to inflation, such rising labor costs and a protracted permitting process, are beyond control once
the decision to invest is made.
Minas Rio employs 12,500 full-time workers, from truck drivers to geologists. Inflation in Brazil is running at
over 5%, in part reflecting the upcoming 2014 World Cup and 2016 Rio de Janeiro Summer Olympics, which
have overheated the construction sector. "We've seen labor costs rise, like what happened in Australia," said
Anglo spokesman James Wyatt-Tilby, referring to the wage and salary spikes that accompanied the recent
mining boom in Australia.
In addition, Anglo has had to obtain "hundreds of permits," said Mr. Wyatt-Tilby. In March, a judge in Brazil
ordered Anglo to stop construction while Brazil's historical-conservation agency excavated and reviewed
artifacts on site, a process that was completed in September.
"What killed Anglo is that they had to keep contractors [at Minas Rio during delays] so they wouldn't go
somewhere else," says Ben Davis, an analyst with Liberum Capital.

Some Investors Likely to Face New Tax Bite --- Those Dabbling in Real Estate Could See Surtax on Rental Income Starting Jan. 1; Just Who...
The Property Report
Some Investors Likely to Face New Tax Bite --- Those Dabbling in Real Estate Could See Surtax on
Rental Income Starting Jan. 1; Just Who Qualifies Is in Question
By A.D. Pruitt
1,064 words
31 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
Passive real-estate investors will be subject to a new 3.8% surtax on Jan. 1 on rental income if they are part
of investment partnerships like an umbrella partnership real-estate investment trust, or Upreit, or 1031
exchange where real-estate owners defer capital-gains taxes on the sale of property by swapping it for a
similar property type. The tax, targeted to high-income individuals, is intended to pay for Medicare under
health-care overhaul. However, investors can defer taxes on capital gains that are subject to this tax by
exchanging their properties under these vehicles. A Property Report article on Wednesday incorrectly said
that investors would be able to delay the Medicare tax on rental income under an Upreit and 1031 exchange.
(WSJ Nov. 3, 2012)
Subscribe to WSJ: http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires
(END)
The presidential election could determine whether the wealthiest Americans will see their income taxes rise or
fall. But there is one tax increase that is likely to occur no matter who wins office: an investment surtax that
begins on New Year's Day and will hit thousands of real-estate investors.
The tax won't affect real-estate companies or those who work full-time managing their real-estate portfolio.
But so-called passive investors -- those who dabble in real estate part-time but have other jobs -- could be
subject to a 3.8% surtax on rental income from the properties.
The tax, which was included in the Affordable Healthcare Act passed by Congress in 2010, is intended to
raise funds to pay for the law. It covers all types of investment income, not just real-estate income. But the
rules regarding real-estate investors, including guidelines that determine who is and isn't a passive investor,
remain in contention. And that is causing confusion and anxiety.
"Some of the confusion is whether existing rules will apply to rental activities" for purposes of the new tax,
said Jeanne Sullivan, a director in the Washington National Tax office of KPMG, referring to a handful of
longstanding provisions relating to real-estate professionals under the current tax code.
Real-estate investors could get relief from the tax if Republican presidential candidate Mitt Romney is elected
and makes good on his pledge to repeal the health law. But that likely would require Republicans to retake
control of the Senate, and it isn't clear the tax changes could be wiped away immediately, if at all.
High-paid professionals who have full-time jobs and invest in real estate on the side -- a group that includes
many doctors, lawyers, dentists and those who work in finance -- make up a large percentage of those
expected to get snagged under the new law.
Many of these investors say they will pay the levy, especially since there are few other investments that have
returns are high as real estate. "U.S. government bonds are not providing any yield . . . corporate bonds are
cheap, gold's at an all-time high, the Fed is printing money like crazy," said Dan Fasulo, who owns three
small apartment buildings in the Bronx, N.Y., and is a managing director at Real Capital Analytics, a
real-estate research firm in New York. "If you really look at everything, real estate seems like a safe place to
park your money,"
The tax applies to income received from rental properties for single individuals whose net income exceeds
$200,000, and $250,000 for married couples. A nuance of the code applies the tax to either a person's net
investment income or the amount the adjusted gross income exceeds these thresholds, whichever is less.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A key exemption includes people who can verify that they spend over 500 hours a year, or roughly two hours
a day, managing or operating their properties or who are real-estate professionals managing rental properties
as a business or for their livelihood. This pool of eligible tax payers is small.
"Your dentists, your doctors, your lawyers. . .they're not going to be able to meet" these tests, said Thomas
Nice, a tax partner at public accounting firm CohnReznick in Washington. "It's going to be very difficult if they
are working a full-time job to be able to qualify as a real-estate professional under this" code.
But it isn't clear whether everyone who can prove they work over 500 hours a year on their properties will be
exempt. "It's all up for interpretation at this point," said James Guarino, a tax partner at Moody, Famiglietti &
Andronico. "Right now, we're hoping for some kind of guidance from the [Internal Revenue Service] in this
area."
There are, however, two viable options for investors to defer taxes on their rental income, according to tax
experts. One, they can fold their properties into an umbrella partnership real-estate investment trust,
commonly referred to as an Upreit. Under this structure, investors will hand over their properties to a REIT for
a specified period for the company to manage and operate. Or, they can sell their buildings in a 1031
exchange, where real-estate owners defer capital-gains taxes on the sale of property by turning around and
purchasing a similar type of property.
Mr. Nice notes major pitfalls to both strategies. Under the Upreit option, investors risk losing control of their
properties, and under a 1031 exchange, investors have to scramble to find a replacement property in a short
window of time.
Claudia Hill, president of Tax Mam Inc., is advising her clients to understand the law and to decide if their
properties are such a good investment that it is worth the tax.
And she is taking her own advice. Ms. Hill receives rental income from a Home Depot building she partly
owns through an investment partnership near her home, and she also rents out a single-family house.
The tax "isn't enough in my view to cause me to get out of the investment," she said.
"I can tell you right now the return that I'm getting on my rental property is as greater than what I'm getting in
the bank account."

Ben Bernanke: Currency Manipulator
Americas
Ben Bernanke: Currency Manipulator
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
869 words
29 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar is our currency, but it's your problem.
-- U.S. Treasury Secretary John Connally, 1971
In the final televised presidential debate, Mitt Romney promised that if he is elected on Nov. 6 he will "label
China a currency manipulator" on "day one" of his presidency. He also pledged to pay more attention to trade
with Latin America, noting that the region's "economy is almost as big as the economy of China."
To be consistent, Mr. Romney should call out the Federal Reserve on day two for engaging in its own
currency manipulation by way of "quantitative easing," which undermines the value of the dollar relative to
Latin American currencies. After all, no one can expect a healthy trade relationship with the region if the Fed
is goading U.S. trading partners into competitive currency devaluations.
But that's not the main reason why a new U.S. president should want to rein in the Fed. The greater worry is
the one that International Monetary Fund Managing Director Christine Lagarde warned about at the IMF's
October meeting in Tokyo. Easy money from the central banks of developed countries, she said, creates the
risk of "asset price bubbles" in emerging economies.
If history is any guide, such bubbles are likely to lead to financial crises that in turn lead to setbacks in
development. Aside from the damage that does to middle-income countries like Brazil, emerging-market
financial crises also undermine U.S. economic and geopolitical objectives.
From September 2008 through the end of 2011, Mr. Bernanke's Fed created $1.8 trillion in new money. But
Fed policy makers were only warming up. In September they announced that they will engage in a third round
of quantitative easing -- that is, more money creation, ostensibly to spur growth and thus bring down
unemployment -- at a rate of $40 billion per month with no deadline.
With so many dollars sloshing around in U.S. banks and with a fed-funds rate set near zero, investors have
found it hard to earn a decent return. The scavenger hunt for yield has sent dollars rushing into emerging
markets where, as they are converted into local currency, they put upward pressure on the exchange rate.
Brazil has experienced this in spades. Brazilian Finance Minister Guido Mantega has complained bitterly
about it because in his mind the higher relative value of the real makes Brazil worse off.
In Mr. Bernanke's remarks at the IMF meeting in Tokyo, he suggested that emerging economies ought to
simply let their currencies appreciate rather than "resist appreciation" through "currency management." To do
otherwise, he noted, can mean "susceptibility to importing inflation," which means making Brazilians poorer.
Mr. Bernanke has a point. The closed, heavily regulated Brazilian economy is held back by too much
government, not a strong real.
Indeed, the quest for a weak currency to boost exports is counterproductive if the goal is development. As
former Salvadoran Finance Minister Manuel Hinds wrote earlier this month for the Atlantic magazine's new
online publication, Quartz, the Brazilian boom in industrial production, which stirred "the idea that [Brazil]
would become the engine of the world," came from "the inflow of dollars that Mr. Mantega hates so much."
But Mr. Bernanke's dismissive posture toward emerging economies missed the larger point. As Mr. Hinds
also pointed out, "the exceptional prosperity would last only as long as the dollars kept on coming." And
there's the rub. The boom is an artificially high valuation of the Brazilian economy, produced only because Mr.
Bernanke has made the world awash in dollars.

--- Page 109 ---
Page 109 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The sustainability issue is troubling. As Bank of England Governor Mervyn King noted in a speech last week:
"When the factors leading to a downturn are long-lasting, only continual injections of [monetary] stimulus will
suffice to sustain the level of real activity. Obviously, this cannot continue indefinitely."
In a perfect world, the end of the dollar flows -- or a downturn in soaring commodity prices when investor
expectations begin to shift -- would simply mean an economic slowdown. But booms are almost always
accompanied by credit expansions, and Brazil's is no different. Since 2004, bank credit has grown to 167% of
gross domestic product from 97%.
What happens when a leveraged economy, living on accommodative monetary policy, suddenly finds the
spigot turned off? Ask Americans who were on the receiving end of Fed tightening in 2007.
In Tokyo, Mr. Bernanke spoke to the world the way former U.S. Treasury Secretary John Connally spoke to
the G-10 in Rome in 1971 after the U.S. abandoned the Bretton Woods agreement that had tied the dollar to
gold: Get over it. We do what we want.
That attitude wasn't constructive for Americans or the rest of the world. If some future U.S. president intends
to restore American prestige in economic leadership, restoring Fed credibility as a responsible manager of
the world's reserve currency is a necessary first step.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Fed's Leverage Risk Haunts U.S. Economy" -- WSJ Oct. 31, 2012)

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Here's a Tip: Watch Those Pricey TIPS
By Ben Levisohn
741 words
27 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal

English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bond investors are flocking to a classic inflation hedge to protect their portfolios from higher consumer
prices. But the demand has been so strong that some strategists are urging caution.
On Sept. 13, the Federal Reserve announced it would start a new round of bond buying to spur the economy.
Investors concluded the monetary stimulus would spark inflation, and piled into Treasury inflation-protected
securities, or TIPS.
The rush has made the already pricey bonds even pricier, experts say.
"Investors buy TIPS for inflation protection," says Paul Twitchell, global head of event strategies at
Minneapolis-based hedge fund Whitebox Advisors, which oversees about $2.3 billion. "But they're so
expensive now that they aren't protecting anything."
TIPS are Treasurys that are linked to inflation. Investors receive the bond's "coupon" -- its interest payment
-- plus a percentage equal to the growth of the consumer-price index.
These days, coupons are low. A TIPS maturing on July 15, 2022, pays just 0.13%. And because the price
has shot up, the yield, which moves in the opposite direction, has fallen to minus-0.66%, not including the CPI
adjustment.
"Owning TIPS made sense when real yields were higher," says John Brynjolfsson, chief investment officer at
hedge fund Armored Wolf in Irvine, Calif, which manages about $735 million. "But I have a hard time justifying
a negative real yield."
One measure of a TIPS's value is its "break-even" point, or the amount an investor needs prices to rise to
match the performance of a plain-vanilla Treasury. The break-even for 10-year TIPS jumped to 2.64% the
day after the Fed announcement, near the highest level since April 2011.
The break-even has since fallen to 2.46%. But it remains much higher than the CPI, which is about 2%. If the
trend persists, 10-year TIPS will underperform regular Treasurys.
Recent history offers more cause for concern. Since the beginning of 2003, the BarclaysU.S.
TreasuryInflation Protected Notes Index has returned just 3.9% during the 12 months after the TIPS
break-even was 2.46% or higher, according to an analysis of FactSet data -- far below the average 12-month
return of 6.7%.
The winner of the presidential election on Nov. 6 also could affect the TIPS market, says Kevin Kearns, a
portfolio manager at Loomis Sayles who helps oversee about $6 billion. That is because Fed Chairman Ben
Bernanke's term expires on Jan. 31, 2014. Republican candidate Mitt Romney would be more likely to
nominate someone less willing to implement large-scale bond buying, while President Barack Obama would
be more likely to choose someone who would continue the current policies, Mr. Kearns says.
Some inflation-fighting mutual funds limit the use of TIPS in favor of derivatives called "inflation swaps." The
$1.5 billion J.P. MorganInflation Managed Bond Fund, for one, layers such derivatives to add inflation
protection to an actively managed portfolio of bonds, which its managers expect to generate more return than
TIPS over the long-term. The fund has returned 4.7% so far in 2012, and charges a management fee of
0.75%.
Others recommend looking beyond bonds. Stocks aren't a true hedge against inflation, but they have
produced positive returns on average after adjusting for rising prices in all but the worst inflation
environments, according to research from Elroy Dimson, Paul Marsh and Mike Staunton of the London
Business School. Gold, meanwhile, is the only asset among those studied that maintained its value during all
periods of inflation, though with large price swings.

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 187 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors also can consider beaten up commodities and real estate, among others, says Whitebox's Mr.
Twitchell.
Some mutual funds use an array of assets to fight inflation. The Fidelity Strategic Real Return Fund owns
floating-rate loans, real-estate investment trusts and commodities, while the Pimco Inflation Response Multi
Asset Fund owns commodities, REITs and emerging-market currencies, among other assets. The Fidelity
fund has returned 7.8% this year and charges 0.76%. The Pimco fund has returned 8.4% this year and has
an expense ratio of 1.25%.
The upshot, says Adam Patti, CEO of ETF provider IndexIQ: "There are more efficient ways to hedge
inflation risk than just buying TIPS."

Supply Chains in Asia Still Not Flowing Freely
Heard on the Street
Supply Chains in Asia Still Not Flowing Freely
By Aaron Back
323 words
26 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Asia's supply chains still are looking clunky.
Thursday's industrial-production data out of regional bellwethers Thailand and Singapore were weak. Both
countries are key to the Asian value chain, in areas like autos for Thailand and electronics and biotechnology
for Singapore.
In Singapore, September industrial production fell 2.5% from a year earlier, below expectations for a 1.9%
rise. Electronics production was especially weak, down 12.2%. In Thailand, industrial production fell a
whopping 13.7%.
Earlier in the week, sagging Thai imports also were a concern, down 7.7% from a year earlier, pointing to
persistently weak domestic demand. Thai exports eked out a slight gain, but excluding gold and jewelry.
exports were actually down 4.3%, according to DBS.
Amid the gloom, though, there are some positive signs around the region. A pickup in August electronics
imports for the Philippines could be a sign that manufacturers and exporters there are stocking up on
components. Though Thailand's export data were weak overall, vehicles and auto parts bucked the
downtrend.
Nor are policy makers sitting on their hands. The Philippine central bank cut interest rates on Thursday.
Thailand cut rates last week. And quantitative easing in the U.S. is sending liquidity streaming into property
and stock markets across Asia.
Further north in Asia, exports are recovering in Taiwan and South Korea, partly due to a ramp-up in
production of smartphones and tablets. China's September data dump also suggested the economy there
may be bottoming out.
Still, some negative signals out of Southeast Asia are a reminder of the precarious state of global trade.
Euro-zone debt worries and the looming U.S. "fiscal cliff" remain concerns. A misstep on either of these and
Asia's supply chains could quickly jam up again.
